Chapter Three: Disagreement in Chain and Text. Hajjaj ibn Arta'ah: he is truthful, frequent mistake and tadlis. (r) 6. 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar al-'Umari: he is weak. Yahya ibn Sa'id (9) (4) (4). The trustworthy: among them from al-Zuhri from 'Urwah from 'A'ishah, connected, except that after investigation and scrutiny it became clear that the connected narration is an error, and the correct is that it is disconnected between al-Zuhri and 'A'ishah, and the mention of 'Urwah in the chain is an error. And among them are those whose hadith is suitable for follow-up; they all narrated the hadith. So al-Nasa'i said about the connected narration: 'This is an error.' And al-Mizzi explained al-Nasa'i's intent, saying: 'He means that the correct is the hadith of al-Zuhri from 'A'ishah and Hafṣah as mursal.' And al-Tirmidhi also stated that the connected narration is an error and the correct is that it is disconnected, and he mentioned the conclusive evidence for that, saying: 'It is narrated from Ibn Jurayj, he said: I asked al-Zuhri, I said to him: Did 'Urwah narrate to you from 'A'ishah? He said: I did not hear anything from 'Urwah about this, but I heard during the caliphate of Sulayman ibn 'Abd al-Malik from people about some of this that someone asked 'A'ishah about the hadith.' And earlier al-Tirmidhi asked his teacher al-Bukhari, and he said: 'I asked Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari about this hadith, and he said: The hadith of al-Zuhri from 'Urwah from 'A'ishah is not authentic.' And Abu Zur'ah and Abu Hatim al-Raziyyan ruled in favor of the disconnected narration over the connected one. I say: The trustworthy and reliable narrators from among the students of al-Zuhri narrated it as disconnected, and they are eight individuals: 1. Malik ibn Anas, he is trustworthy, an imam more famous than to be described. 2. Ma'mar ibn Rashid, he is trustworthy, reliable, and virtuous. 3. 'Ubaydullah ibn 'Umar al-'Umari, he is trustworthy and reliable. 4. Yunus ibn Yazid al-Ayli, he is trustworthy, one of the reliable. 5. Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, he is trustworthy, a memorizer, a jurist, an imam, a proof. 6. 'Abd al-Malik ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz ibn Jurayj, he is trustworthy, reliable. 7. Muhammad ibn al-Walid al-Zubaydi, he is trustworthy and reliable. 8. Bakr ibn Wail, he is truthful. All of them narrated it from al-Zuhri from 'A'ishah as disconnected, and their narration contradicts the narration of those who narrated it as connected. This indicates that the hadith scholars do not have a fixed rule in such matters; rather, the reference is to contextual evidence and preponderances surrounding the narration.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed case study from the science of hadith (mustalah al-hadith) that illustrates how scholars resolve apparent contradictions between chains of transmission. The author is demonstrating a key methodological point: the decision to prefer one chain over another is not mechanical but depends on contextual evidence (qara'in) and preponderance (tarjih). The hadith in question revolves around whether al-Zuhri narrated from 'Urwah from 'A'ishah (connected) or from 'A'ishah directly without 'Urwah (disconnected/mursal). Multiple major hadith scholars—al-Nasa'i, al-Tirmidhi, al-Bukhari, Abu Zur'ah, Abu Hatim—all concluded that the connected version is erroneous and the correct one is disconnected (mursal). The author then lists eight highly reliable narrators who all transmitted the disconnected version, strengthening the case. The lesson is that the muhaddithun (hadith critics) weigh all available evidence: the reliability of narrators, the number of chains, the statements of the narrators themselves (like al-Zuhri's denial of hearing from 'Urwah), and the opinions of earlier experts. This is a classic example of tarjih (preponderance) when faced with ta'arud (contradiction) in hadith. For the Hanafi usul al-fiqh approach, this methodology is directly relevant: when legal proofs appear contradictory, scholars apply similar principles of tarjih based on strength of evidence.

Key Terms
منقطع (munqati'): A chain with a missing link; here specifically that al-Zuhri did not hear from 'Urwah.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preferring one contradictory narration over another based on preponderating evidence.
قرائن (qara'in): Contextual evidence that helps determine the preponderant narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Zuhri's own statement cause scholars to prefer the disconnected (munqati') version?
A) Because al-Zuhri was an imam.
B) Because he explicitly said he did not hear the hadith from 'Urwah.
C) Because 'Urwah was weak.
D) Because the connected chain had many narrators.

Key Takeaway
Preferring a disconnected chain over a connected one is justified when the narrator himself denies hearing from the intermediate link, supported by multiple reliable narrators.

The Hadith of Aisha and Hafsa's Fast: Chain Dispute and Its Effect on Legal Evidence
Analyzing the correct chain for the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct chain of a hadith by applying criteria of shudhudh and 'illah, and evaluate the impact of chain differences on the hadith's evidentiary value.

Core Idea
A hadith's authenticity is not guaranteed by a trustworthy narrator alone; chain criticism reveals hidden errors.

In this passage, we examine a renowned dispute over the chain of a hadith about Aisha and Hafsa fasting. The disagreement between hadith masters and Ibn Hazm illustrates how careful chain analysis (naqd al-isnad) determines whether a report is accepted as evidence. You will learn the principles of shudhudh (irregularity) and 'illah (hidden defect), and how consensus of critics outweighs the trustworthiness of a single narrator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing chains
Read the passage and locate the two chains: one via Jarir ibn Hazim with Amra, and the other (considered correct) mursal via al-Zuhri. Note the narrators in each.
The mursal chain is from Yahya ibn Sa'id directly from al-Zuhri, without Amra.
2
Understand the criticism by the four imams
Identify why Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ali ibn al-Madini, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Nasa'i considered Jarir's chain a mistake. They argued that the correct chain is mursal and that Jarir erred.
Their consensus is based on thorough comparison of chains.
3
Examine Ibn Hazm's counterargument
Ibn Hazm defended Jarir, arguing that trustworthiness negates the claim of error unless proof is provided, and that singular narration is not a defect.
Ibn Hazm's position reflects a stricter requirement for declaring a hadith defective.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The text responds that trustworthiness does not prevent error, and the consensus of four imams is a strong indication. It also cites the counter-narration by Hammad ibn Zaid who omitted Amra.
The Hanafi approach prioritizes the majority version and expert consensus.
5
Assess the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if Jarir's chain is accepted, the hadith is considered sound; if mursal, it is weaker. This affects whether the hadith can be used as evidence for rulings on fasting.
The dispute shows how chain differences impact legal inference.

الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن (٢) وللحديث طريق أخرى (١)‏، فقد أخرجه النسائي (٢)‏، والطحاوي (٣)‏، وابن حبان (٤)‏، وابن حزم في المحلى (٥)‏، من طريق جرير بن حازم (٦)‏، عن يحيى بن سعيد (٧)‏، عن عمرة (٨)‏، عن عائشة. هكذا الرواية وظاهرها الصحة، إلا أن جهابذة المحدّثين قد عدوا غلطاً من جرير بن حازم خطأه في هذا أحمد بن حنبل، وعلي بن المديني، والبيهقي، قال البيهقي: والمحفوظ عن يحيى بن سعيد، عن الزهري، عن عائشة مرسلاً (٩)‏. (١١) ثم أسند البيهقي إلى أحمد بن منصور الرمادي (١١) قال: قلت لعلي بن المديني: يا أبا الحسن تحفظ عن يحيى بن سعيد، عن عمرة، عن عائشة قالت: أصبحت أنا وحفصة صائمتين. فقال لي: من روى هذا؟ قلت ابن وهب عن جرير بن حازم، عن يحيى بن سعيد قال فضحك فقال: مثلك يقول هذا، حدثنا حماد بن زيد عن يحيى بن سعيد، عن الزهري: أن عائشة وحفصة أصبحتا صائمتين. (١٢) وقد أشار النسائي كذلك إلى خطأ فهؤلاء أربعة من أئمة الحديث أشاروا إلى خطأ جرير بن حازم في هذا الحديث، وعدم إقامته لإسناده. ولم يرتض ابن حزم على هذه التخطئة، وأجاب عن ذلك فقال: «لم يتحقق علينا قول من قال أن جرير بن حازم أخطأ في هذا الخبر إلا أن هذا ليس بشيء؛ لأن جريراً ودعوى الخطأ باطل إلا أن يقيم المدعي له برهاناً على صحة دعواه، وليس انفراد ثقة، جرير بإسناده علة؛ لأنه ثقة» (١)‏. (٢) ويجاب على كلام ابن حزم بأن ليس كل ما رواه الثقة صحيحاً، بل يكون فيه الصحيح وغير ذلك؛ لذا فإن الشذوذ والعلة إنما يكونان في حديث الثقة؛ فالعلة إذن معرفة الخطأ في أحاديث الثقات، ثم إن إطباق أربعة من أئمة الحديث على خطأ جرير، لم يكن أمراً اعتباطياً، وإنما قالوا هذا بعد النظر الثاقب والتفتيش والموازنة والمقارنة. أما إقامة الدليل على كل حكم في إعلال الأحاديث، فهذا ربما لا يستطيع الجهبذ الناقد أن يعبر عنه إنما هو شيء ينقدح في نفسه تعجز عبارته عنه ثم إن التفرد ليس علة كما سبق أن فصلنا القول فيه في مبحث التفرد، وإنما هو مُلقٍ لِلضوء على العِلّة ومواقع الخلل وكوامن الخطأ، ثم إنا وجدنا الدليل على خطأ جرير بن حازم إذ قد خالفه الإمام الثقة الثبت حماد بن زيد (٣)‏، فرواه عن يحيى بن سعيد ولم يذكر عمرة (٤)‏. وللحديث طريق أخرى فقد أخرجه الطبراني (٥)‏ من طريق: يعقوب بن الزهري، قال: حدثنا هشام بن عبد الله بن عكرمة بن عبد الرحمن، عن الحارث بن هشام، عن هشام بن عروة، عن أبيه، عن عائشة. قال الطبراني عقب روايته له: «لم يرو هذا الحديث عن هشام بن عروة إلا هشام ابن عكرمة تفرد به يعقوب بن مُحَمَّد الزهري». قلت هذه الرواية ضعيفة لا تصلح للمتابعة، إذ فيها علتان: الأولى: يعقوب بن مُحَمَّد الزهري، فيه كلام ليس باليسير، فقد قال فيه الإمام أحمد: «ليس بشيء»، وَقَالَ مرة: لا يساوي حديثه شيئاً»، وَقَالَ الساجي: «منكر الحديث」(٦)‏. والثانية هشام بن عبد الله بن عكرمة، قال ابن حبان: ينفرد عن هشام بن عروة بما لا أصل له من حديثه – كأنه هشام آخر - لا يعجبني الاحتجاج بخبره إذا
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text (2) The hadith has another chain: it was recorded by al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi, Ibn Hibban, and Ibn Hazm in al-Muhalla, via Jarir ibn Hazim, from Yahya ibn Sa'id, from Amra, from Aisha. Thus the narration, and apparently it is sound, except that the master hadith scholars considered it a mistake from Jarir ibn Hazim. Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ali ibn al-Madini, and al-Bayhaqi declared his error. Al-Bayhaqi said: 'What is preserved is from Yahya ibn Sa'id, from al-Zuhri, from Aisha, as mursal.' Then al-Bayhaqi attributed to Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ramadi who said: I said to Ali ibn al-Madini: O Abu al-Hasan, do you preserve from Yahya ibn Sa'id, from Amra, from Aisha that she said: 'I and Hafsa woke up fasting'? He said to me: Who narrated this? I said: Ibn Wahb from Jarir ibn Hazim from Yahya ibn Sa'id. He laughed and said: Someone like you says this? Hammad ibn Zaid narrated to us from Yahya ibn Sa'id, from al-Zuhri: that Aisha and Hafsa woke up fasting. And al-Nasa'i also indicated the error. So these four imams of hadith indicated the error of Jarir ibn Hazim in this hadith and his failure to establish its chain. Ibn Hazm did not approve of this criticism and responded: 'The statement of one who said that Jarir ibn Hazim made a mistake in this report is not established against us, except that this is nothing; because Jarir... and the claim of error is invalid unless the claimant provides proof for his claim, and the singularity of a trustworthy narrator is not a defect because Jarir's chain is sound because he is trustworthy.' And it is responded to Ibn Hazm's statement that not everything narrated by a trustworthy narrator is sound; rather, among his narrations are sound and otherwise. Therefore, irregularity and defect occur in the hadith of a trustworthy narrator; the defect is thus knowledge of error in the hadith of trustworthy narrators. Moreover, the agreement of four imams of hadith on Jarir's error was not arbitrary, but they said this after penetrating examination, investigation, balancing, and comparison. As for providing evidence for every ruling in the criticism of hadith, perhaps the expert critic cannot express it; it is something that occurs to his mind that his expression cannot articulate. Also, singularity is not a defect as we have previously detailed in the section on singularity; rather, it throws light on the defect, locations of weakness, and hidden errors. Then we found evidence for Jarir ibn Hazim's error because the trustworthy, reliable imam Hammad ibn Zaid opposed him, narrating it from Yahya ibn Sa'id without mentioning Amra. And the hadith has another chain: al-Tabarani recorded it via Ya'qub ibn al-Zuhri, who said: Hisham ibn Abdullah ibn Ikrima ibn Abd al-Rahman narrated to us from al-Harith ibn Hisham from Hisham ibn Urwa from his father from Aisha. Al-Tabarani said after his narration: 'This hadith was not narrated from Hisham ibn Urwa except by Hisham ibn Ikrima; Ya'qub ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri alone narrated it.' I say this narration is weak, not suitable for follow-up, because it has two defects: first: Ya'qub ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri, there is considerable criticism about him; Imam Ahmad said: 'He is nothing,' and once said: 'His hadith is not worth anything,' and al-Saji said: 'He is denounced in hadith.' Second: Hisham ibn Abdullah ibn Ikrima; Ibn Hibban said: 'He narrates from Hisham ibn Urwa what has no basis in his hadith—as if he is another Hisham—I do not like to use his report as evidence when'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the correct chain for the hadith where Aisha and Hafsa woke up fasting. The author presents two main chains and assesses their authenticity. The first chain, via Jarir ibn Hazim from Yahya ibn Sa'id from Amra from Aisha, appears sound but is criticized by four major hadith imams (Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ali ibn al-Madini, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Nasa'i) who all state that the correct chain is mursal—from Yahya ibn Sa'id from al-Zuhri from Aisha without Amra. The author then presents Ibn Hazm's defense, which argues that Jarir is trustworthy and his singular narration is not a defect. However, the author refutes this by explaining that trustworthiness does not guarantee immunity from error, and the consensus of critics is based on careful analysis. Furthermore, the author points out that Hammad ibn Zaid, a more reliable narrator, contradicts Jarir by narrating without Amra. The passage then examines a second chain from al-Tabarani, which is deemed weak due to two unreliable narrators: Ya'qub ibn Muhammad al-Zuhri and Hisham ibn Abdullah ibn Ikrima. Thus, the hadith is not proven to be authentic through any chain, highlighting the importance of chain criticism in determining legal rulings.

Key Terms
شذوذ (shudhudh): Irregularity in a hadith when a trustworthy narrator contradicts a more reliable narrator.
علة (illa): A hidden defect that impairs the authenticity of a hadith, often found in trustworthy narrators.
انفراد (infirad): When a narrator is the sole transmitter of a hadith; not necessarily a defect but can indicate a hidden flaw.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the four imams of hadith consider Jarir ibn Hazim's chain of the hadith to be a mistake?
A) Because Jarir was a weak narrator
B) Because the correct chain is from Yahya ibn Sa'id from al-Zuhri without mentioning Amra
C) Because Amra never narrated from Aisha
D) Because the hadith contradicts the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Chain criticism (naqd al-isnad) is essential for authenticating hadith; a trustworthy narrator can err, and consensus of critics is a powerful tool to identify such errors.

Weakness of the Hadith on Breaking Voluntary Fast and Its Effect on Fiqh Disagreement
Critiquing hadith chains and presenting the first juristic opinion on make-up of voluntary worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the weaknesses in the chains of the hadith on breaking a voluntary fast and state the first opinion on the obligation of making up a voluntary act.

Core Idea
A weak hadith cannot support an obligation; the first opinion on make-up relies on other principles.

This passage demonstrates how a weak hadith—the hadith of Aisha and Hafsa breaking a fast—influences juristic opinions. The author shows that all chains are weak due to narrator weakness or disconnection. He then presents the first of three opinions: that commencing a supererogatory act makes it wajib, necessitating qada' if broken. This sets the stage for the Hanafi position which may differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chains
List each chain given (via Khusayf, Hammad ibn al-Walid, Muhammad ibn Abi Salamah). Note the full sequence of narrators for each.
The original chain via al-Zuhri is also mentioned as being both muttasil and munqati' in earlier discussion.
2
Evaluate each chain for weakness
For each chain, identify the specific weakness: a weak narrator or disconnection (inqita'). Use the citations from hadith critics (Ahmad, Abu Hatim, etc.) given in the text.
Disconnection is a fatal flaw that cannot be cured by multiple weak chains.
3
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that the hadith is weak overall due to disconnection and the weakness of its other chains. Multiple weak routes do not strengthen it.
This is a key principle in hadith criticism: severe weakness (like disconnection) prevents strengthening.
4
Connect to fiqh
The author transitions to the juristic disagreement. The first opinion holds that commencing a voluntary act makes it binding, so breaking it requires make-up. Note that this opinion may rely on the weak hadith or other principles.
The Hanafi position (to be presented later) may differ based on stronger evidence.

Chapter Three: Disagreement in Isnad and Matn
And the hadith has another chain: Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated it from the route of Khusayf ibn Abd al-Rahman, from Sa'id ibn Jubayr, that A'isha and Hafsa ... the hadith. And it is a weak chain due to the weakness of Khusayf ibn Abd al-Rahman; Imam Ahmad, Abu Hatim, and Yahya al-Qattan declared him weak, although some strengthened him.
And the hadith has another chain: al-Bazzar and al-Tabarani narrated it from the route of Hammad ibn al-Walid, from Ubayd Allah ibn Umar, from Nafi', from Ibn Umar ... the hadith. And it is a weak chain; al-Haythami said: In it is Hammad ibn al-Walid, whom the imams declared weak.
And the hadith has another chain: al-Uqayli and al-Tabarani narrated it from the route of Muhammad ibn Abi Salamah al-Makki, from Muhammad ibn Amr, from Abu Salamah, from Abu Hurayrah, who said: A gift was given ... the hadith. And it is a weak chain; al-Haythami said: In it is Muhammad ibn Abi Salamah al-Makki, and he was declared weak due to this hadith.
Conclusion: The hadith is not authentic as a continuous chain, and it does not fulfill the conditions of soundness; it is a weak hadith due to its disconnection and the weakness of its other chains.
The effect of this hadith on the disagreement of jurists: the ruling on one who breaks a voluntary fast.
And since we have spoken at length about the hadith of al-Zuhri as continuous and disconnected, and we have mentioned its chains and supporting witnesses, and we have shown the weakness and defect in them, I will now speak about the effect of this hadith on the disagreement of jurists. I say: Whoever begins a voluntary fast or a voluntary prayer and does not complete his supererogatory act, is he obligated to make it up or not?
The jurists differed on this into three opinions:
The first opinion:
Some scholars held that the supererogatory act becomes obligatory upon the responsible person by commencing it, so if he invalidates it, he must make it up, whether it is a fast, a prayer, or anything else. This is narrated from Ibn Abbas, Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, Anas ibn Sirin, 'Ata', Mujahid, al-Thawri, and Abu Thawr.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a case study in the effect of hadith criticism on juristic disagreement. The author begins by examining multiple chains of a hadith concerning Aisha and Hafsa breaking a voluntary fast. He demonstrates that every chain is weak due to either a weak narrator or disconnection (inqita'). The conclusion is that the hadith is not authentic as a continuous (muttasil) narration; it remains weak because of its disconnection and the weakness of its supporting chains. The author then transitions to the fiqh dispute that this hadith influences: whether one who begins a voluntary act of worship (such as fast or prayer) is obligated to complete it or make it up if broken. He presents the first of three opinions: that commencing a supererogatory act makes it binding (wajib), so if one invalidates it, make-up (qada') is required. This view is attributed to several early authorities including Ibn Abbas and al-Hasan al-Basri. The passage does not yet give the Hanafi position, but the discussion sets the stage for the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
ضعف (ḍu'f): Weakness in a hadith chain due to narrator deficiency or disconnection.
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): Disconnection in the chain of transmission, making the hadith munqati' (broken).
صوم التطوع (ṣawm al-taṭawwuʿ): A voluntary fast, not obligatory by original shari'ah ruling.
القضاء (al-qaḍāʾ): Making up an act of worship after it has been invalidated or missed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It becomes obligatory to make it up because the act became binding by commencing.
C) No make-up is required because it was voluntary.

Key Takeaway
The hadith on breaking a voluntary fast is weak due to disconnection and weak narrators; thus it cannot reliably support the obligation of make-up.

How Sanad and Matn Differences Affect the Ruling on Breaking a Voluntary Fast
Analyzing the role of hadith in a fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the impact of hadith sanad and matn discrepancies on the Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Hanbali rulings for breaking a voluntary fast, and apply the Hanafi usul principle of prioritizing sahih hadith over weak ones.

Core Idea
Hadith authenticity (sanad) and textual interpretation (matn) directly determine whether a voluntary fast can be broken without qada'.

This passage demonstrates how textual criticism of hadith leads to ikhtilaf among madhhabs. The Shafi'is and Hanbalis rely on authentic hadiths that permit breaking a voluntary fast without qada', while the Hanafis hold a different view based on other evidence. By analyzing the sanad and matn, you will see how a single issue—breaking a voluntary fast—can produce different rulings depending on which hadiths are accepted and how they are interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue and Opposing Views
The passage discusses breaking a voluntary fast (sawm al-nafl). Note which madhhabs are mentioned (Shafi'is, Hanbalis, Malikis) and their basic positions. The Hanafi view is not detailed here but is known from other sources.
Later in the passage, the Maliki view is introduced but cut off.
2
Examine the Hadiths Cited (Sanad and Matn)
Three hadiths are presented: (1) Aisha's hadith of the Prophet breaking his fast, (2) Abu Juhaifa's hadith of Salman and Abu al-Darda, (3) Umm Hani's hadith. For each, note the sanad (chain) and matn (text). Consider the authenticity claimed and any weakness mentioned (e.g., Tirmidhi notes weakness in Umm Hani's chain).
These hadiths are used to support the Shafi'i/Hanbali view.
3
Analyze the Counter-Hadith (al-Zuhri)
The text mentions that the hadith of al-Zuhri is considered weak and disconnected (munqati') by the opposing school. This is why they discard it. Identify which hadith this might be (likely the one Hanafis rely on) and understand that its weakness changes the evidentiary balance.
The exact content of al-Zuhri's hadith is not provided.
4
Observe the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: For Shafi'is, breaking a voluntary fast does not require qada'; for Hanbalis (according to the passage) it does not either (though traditional Hanbali view may differ). For Hanafis, qada' is required. Understand that this difference stems from which hadiths are deemed reliable and how they are interpreted.
The passage groups Shafi'is and Hanbalis together; historically they differ, but here they are presented as sharing the same proof.
5
Consider the Hanafi Response (Outside the Passage)
Although not in the text, Hanafis argue that a voluntary fast becomes binding once started, based on other hadiths (e.g., 'Whoever starts a voluntary fast then breaks it without excuse, let him make it up' or qiyas with obligatory fast). They may also reject the hadiths cited here as weak or specific to the Prophet.
This step requires external knowledge but is essential for a balanced evaluation.

Ibn Abbas (1), Jabir ibn Abdullah (3). And Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Mujahid (4), al-Thawri (5), and Ishaq (6). And it is the madhhab of the Shafi'is (7) and the Hanbalis (8). Then he came to us another day, and we said, 'O Messenger of Allah, we have been gifted some hais (10).' He said, 'Show it to me. I started the day fasting.' Then he ate. Narrated by Muslim.
2. From Abu Juhaifa (1) who said: 'The Prophet paired Salman (2) and Abu al-Darda in brotherhood. Salman visited Abu al-Darda and saw Umm al-Darda (3) in a disheveled state (4). He asked her, 'What is the matter?' She said, 'Your brother Abu al-Darda has no need for this world.' Then Abu al-Darda came and prepared food for him. He said, 'Eat.' Salman said, 'I am fasting.' Then he got up again to pray, and Salman said, 'Sleep.' When it was the last part of the night, Salman said, 'Get up now,' and they prayed. Then Salman said to him, 'Indeed, your Lord has a right over you, your self has a right over you, and your family has a right over you. Give every rightful due his right.' He went to the Prophet and mentioned it, and the Prophet said, 'Salman has spoken the truth.' Narrated by al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Khuzayma, and al-Bayhaqi.
These are authentic hadiths that permit the voluntary faster to break his fast and do not command him to make it up.
Hadith of Umm Hani' from the Prophet: 'The voluntary faster is the trustee of himself; if he wishes, he may fast, and if he wishes, he may break.' Narrated by Imam Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi. Al-Tirmidhi said, 'In its chain there is discussion.'
The third opinion:
The detail, which is the madhhab of the Malikis. They said: If he breaks due to an excuse, it is permissible; if he breaks without an excuse...

Commentary
This passage provides a textual analysis of how differences in hadith chains (sanad) and texts (matn) lead to divergent fiqh rulings, specifically on the issue of breaking a voluntary fast (sawm al-nafl). The author presents the second opinion—attributed to the Shafi'is and Hanbalis—along with its proofs. The key proof is that the hadith of al-Zuhri (which might have indicated otherwise) is considered weak and disconnected (munqati'). Therefore, they rely on several authentic hadiths. The first hadith from Aisha shows the Prophet himself broke his voluntary fast when food was presented, without making up the fast. The second hadith from Abu Juhaifa illustrates Salman convincing Abu al-Darda to break his fast, and the Prophet endorsed Salman's advice. The third hadith from Umm Hani' explicitly states that the voluntary faster is 'trustee of himself,' meaning he has the choice to continue or break. Al-Tirmidhi noted a weakness in its chain, but the author cites it nonetheless. After presenting these hadiths, the author summarizes that they permit breaking and do not require qada'. Finally, the third opinion (Maliki) is introduced: they distinguish between breaking with an excuse (permissible) and without an excuse (ruling incomplete in text). The Hanafi view is not discussed in this passage, but typically Hanafis hold that a voluntary fast, once started, becomes binding and requires qada' if broken (though not kaffarah). This example demonstrates how critical evaluation of hadith (sanad and matn) directly impacts fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
صائم النفل (sa'im al-nafl): One who is engaged in a voluntary (supererogatory) fast, as opposed to an obligatory fast.
أمين نفسه (ameen nafsihi): Trustee of himself; meaning the voluntary faster has authority over his own fast and may choose to continue or break it.
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs a voluntary act of worship; here specifically the voluntary faster.

Breaking a Voluntary Fast: Hadith Evidence and Usul Dispute
Hadith evidence and inter-madhhab dispute on voluntary fast breaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Maliki positions on breaking a voluntary fast, and apply the principle of dalalah (implication) from the hadith to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
A voluntary faster may break his fast without qada; the hadith show no command for makeup, while the Maliki view conditions it on an excuse.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: how do we derive the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast from the hadith? The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning (zahir) of the hadith that allows breaking without qada, while the Maliki school introduces a distinction based on the presence of an excuse. We will analyse the hadith evidence and the interpretive principles that lead to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith evidence
Locate the two hadith: the story of Salman and Abu al-Darda' (Abu Juhayfa) and the hadith of Umm Hani. Note their reliability as per the authors mentioned.
One is sound (sahih), the other has chain discussion (maqal).
2
Classify the proof type
Determine whether the hadith are explicit (nass) or imply permissibility. The Umm Hani hadith directly says 'if he wishes he may break'; the Abu Juhayfa hadith shows prophetic approval (taqrir) of breaking without qada.
Both are ahad (zanni al-thubut) but still authoritative.
3
Apply the Hanafi usul principle
For the Hanafi school, the zahir (apparent meaning) of permissibility is taken. The prophetic taqrir and explicit wording indicate that the act is allowed without any condition. No command to make up is present, so qada is not required.
The principle of al-ibaha (permissibility) governs.
4
Contrast the Maliki view
The Maliki school introduces a condition: breaking without an excuse requires qada. This suggests they analogize (qiyas) voluntary fast to obligatory fast in terms of invalidation, or they interpret the hadith to apply only when there is a reason.
The difference stems from whether the hadith is taken as unconditional.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
The practical result (thamarah) is that Hanafis do not require qada, Malikis do if no excuse. The dispute is based on different readings of the same dalil and varying weight given to general permissibility versus qiyas.
Both views are legitimate usul positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢. عن أبي جحيفة قال: «آخى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بين سلمان وأبي الدرداء، فزار سلمان أبا الدرداء، فرأى أم الدرداء متبذلة، فقال لها: ما شأنك؟ قالت: أخوك أبو الدرداء ليس له حاجة في الدنيا فجاء أبو الدرداء فصنع له طعاماً، فقال: كل، قال: فإني صائم، قال: ما أنا بأكل حتى تأكل، قال: فأكل، فلما كان الليل ذهب أبو الدرداء يقوم، فقال: نم، فنام، ثم ذهب يقوم، فقال: نم، فلما كان من آخر الليل، قال سلمان قم الآن، فصليا، فقال له سلمان: إن لربك عليك حقاً، ولنفسك عليك حقاً، ولأهلك عليك حقاً، فأعط كل ذي حق حقه، فأتى النبي فذكر له، فقال النبي: صدق سلمان». أخرجه البخاري، والترمذي، وابن خزيمة، والبيهقي. فهذه أحاديث صحيحة أجازت لصائم النفل الإفطار، ولم تأمره بقضاء. حديث أم هانئ عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: الصائم المتطوع أمين نفسه، إن شاء صام وإن شاء أفطر. أخرجه الإمام أحمد، والترمذي، والنسائي، والدارقطني، والبيهقي. قال الترمذي: «في إسناده مقال». القول الثالث: التفصيل وهو مذهب المالكية قالوا: إن أفطر بعذر جاز، وإن أفطر بغير عذر لزمه القضاء.
2. From Abu Juhayfa: He said: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) established brotherhood between Salman and Abu al-Darda'. Salman visited Abu al-Darda' and saw Umm al-Darda' disheveled. He asked her, 'What is the matter with you?' She said, 'Your brother Abu al-Darda' has no need for the world.' Abu al-Darda' came and prepared food for him. He said, 'Eat.' He said, 'I am fasting.' He said, 'I will not eat until you eat.' So he ate. When night came, Abu al-Darda' got up to pray. He said, 'Sleep.' So he slept. Then he got up again, and he said, 'Sleep.' Then in the last part of the night, Salman said, 'Get up now.' So they prayed. Salman said to him, 'Your Lord has a right over you, and your self has a right over you, and your family has a right over you. Give each right its due.' So he went to the Prophet and mentioned it, and the Prophet said: 'Salman has spoken the truth.'" Narrated by al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Khuzayma, and al-Bayhaqi. These sound hadiths permit the voluntary faster to break his fast and do not command him to make it up. Hadith of Umm Hani from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "The voluntary faster is the trustee of himself; if he wishes he may fast and if he wishes he may break." Narrated by Ahmad, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bayhaqi. Al-Tirmidhi said: "There is discussion in its chain." The third opinion: The detail – it is the Maliki school: They said: If he breaks his fast with an excuse, it is permissible; if he breaks without an excuse, he must make it up.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a fiqh dispute regarding the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast. The author begins by citing two sound hadiths: the first from Abu Juhayfa describing Salman's advice to Abu al-Darda' about balancing rights, which implies that breaking a voluntary fast is permissible without makeup; the second from Umm Hani explicitly stating the voluntary faster may choose. The author then notes that al-Tirmidhi considered the chain of the latter hadith weak. Following this, the third opinion—the Maliki view—is given, which distinguishes between breaking with an excuse (permissible) and without (requiring qada). The Hanafi position, consistent with the first hadiths, is that breaking a voluntary fast is allowed without any requirement to make it up. This is a typical example of how differing hadith interpretations lead to inter-madhhab differences.

Key Terms
صائم النفل (sa'im al-nafl): One who performs a voluntary (supererogatory) fast.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed fast due to invalidation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast according to the hadith discussed?
A) Permissible but requires qada.
B) Permissible without qada.
C) Not permissible.
D) Only permissible with an excuse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a voluntary faster may break his fast without qada, based on explicit prophetic approval and permission, while the Maliki school requires qada if no excuse.

The Fourth Type of Chain Discrepancy: Narrator Through Two Teachers
Defining the fourth type of chain discrepancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth type of chain discrepancy (ikhtilaf al-sanad) and explain its two possible causes (error vs. multiple authentic hearings).

Core Idea
A narrator may transmit the same hadith through two different teachers—either erroneously or authentically from both.

This passage introduces a key category in hadith criticism: when a common narrator reports a hadith via two different intermediaries, both leading to the same Companion. This scenario is not automatically a defect; it may reflect multiple hearing routes. Understanding this type helps us evaluate whether a chain variation indicates a mistake or valid multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of the fourth type
The passage states: a group narrates from a man (the common narrator) from a follower (tabi'i) to a companion, and another group narrates from that same man from a different follower to the same companion. Recognize that the common narrator is the point of divergence.
This type differs from earlier types where the divergence is at the companion level.
2
Recognize the two possible interpretations
The author presents two options: (1) the narrator erred, so only one chain is correct; (2) the narrator heard from both followers and narrated correctly on separate occasions.
The second option preserves the authenticity of both chains.
3
Apply to the given example
The example is the hadith of Zakat al-Fitr narrated from Abu Hurayra via al-Zuhri. One chain goes through Ibn Abi Saghir, another through Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib. Identify the common narrator (al-Zuhri) and the two different followers.
This example shows how the same hadith can be transmitted via two different teachers of al-Zuhri.
4
Understand the relevance to fiqh differences
In Hanafi usul, such chain discrepancies affect the weight of evidence. If the narrator is reliable and heard from both, both chains are acceptable; if it is an error, one chain is discarded. This can lead to differences in rulings when the wording or implicit meaning varies between chains.
The example hadith is used by Hanafis to support the obligation of Zakat al-Fitr on the poor as well as the rich.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع: أن يروي الحديث قوم - مثلاً - عن رجل عن تابعي عن صحابي، ويرويه غيرهم عن ذلك الرجل عن تابعي آخر عن الصحابي بعينه. هذا أحد الأنواع الرئيسة التي تعتري اختلاف الأسانيد، وهو من الاختلافات التي تومئ بعدم ضبط راويها، وتخرج الحديث عن كونه عن رجل إلى رجل آخر، وهنا نقف أمام أمرين وهما: هل أن الراوي أخطأ بهذا الاختلاف فالصواب عن أحدهما والآخر غلط؟ أم أن هذا الراوي سمع الحديث من كلا الرجلين فتارة يحدّث به عن هذا، وتارة يحدّث به عن الآخر، وكلا هذين الراويين قد سمعاه من هذا الصحابي عينه. مثال ذلك: ما أخرجه الدارقطني، من طريق سفيان بن عيينة، عن الزهري، عن ابن أبي صغير، عن أبي هريرة رواية أنه قال: زكاة الفطر على الغني والفقير. فهذا الحديث مما اختلف فيه على الزهري. فقد رواه سفيان بن حسين، عن الزهري، عن سعيد بن المسيب، عن أبي هريرة. والحديث أخرجه عبد الرزاق، وأحمد، والبخاري، والطحاوي.
Fourth type: That a group narrates a hadith from a man from a follower from a companion, and others narrate it from that same man from another follower from the same companion. This is one of the main types that affect chain discrepancies. It is among the differences that indicate a lack of precision of its narrator, and it takes the hadith from being from one man to another. Here we face two matters: Did the narrator err in this difference, so the correct is from one and the other is a mistake? Or did this narrator hear the hadith from both men, sometimes narrating from this one and sometimes from the other, and both of these narrators heard it from this same companion? An example: What al-Daraqutni narrated via Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna from al-Zuhri from Ibn Abi Saghir from Abu Hurayra as a narration that he said: 'Zakat al-Fitr is obligatory upon the rich and the poor.' This hadith is among those where there is difference regarding al-Zuhri. Sufyan ibn Husayn narrated it from al-Zuhri from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib from Abu Hurayra. And the hadith is narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Bukhari, and al-Tahawi.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of chain discrepancy. The situation arises when a hadith is transmitted through one narrator who sometimes reports from one intermediary and sometimes from another, both leading to the same companion. The author then poses two possibilities: either the narrator made an error (only one chain is correct) or he heard from both and narrated correctly on different occasions. The example given is a hadith about Zakat al-Fitr, narrated from al-Zuhri through two different paths: one via Ibn Abi Saghir and another via Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib. This illustrates how a single narrator (al-Zuhri) might have transmitted the same hadith through two different students. The purpose is to highlight how such chain variations can lead to differences in fiqh rulings, as they affect the perceived reliability and wording of the hadith.

Key Terms
اختلاف الأسانيد (ikhtilaf al-asaneed): Discrepancies or differences in the chains of narration of a hadith.
راوي (rawi): Narrator of a hadith; specifically the common link in the chain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A narrator reports from a companion skipping the follower.
B) A narrator reports from two different teachers for the same hadith from the same companion.
C) A narrator reports from a follower instead of a companion.
D) A narrator reports with a different chain ending.

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of chain discrepancy arises when a narrator reports from two different teachers for the same hadith, which may be a sign of error or of multiple authentic hearing.

The Fifth Type of Chain Variation: Addition of a Narrator
Explaining the fifth type of hadith chain discrepancy and its example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an addition of a narrator in a hadith chain is acceptable and when it renders the hadith weak, applying the Hanafi principle of ziyadat al-thiqa.

Core Idea
An addition of a trustworthy narrator in a chain does not impair the hadith; the chain remains valid.

In hadith studies, one of the main causes of discrepancy is the addition or omission of a narrator in the chain. This lesson focuses on the fifth type: when one chain has an extra narrator not found in other chains. We will explore the conditions under which such an addition is acceptable, and when it indicates lack of precision (idtirab). Understanding this is crucial for evaluating the strength of hadith evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the issue
When the same hadith is narrated through multiple chains but one chain has an extra narrator, the common link may be unreliable.
The common link is the narrator from whom all chains diverge.
2
Determine precision of the common link
If the common link is trustworthy and the added narrator is also trustworthy, the addition is typically accepted.
Hanafis accept 'ziyadat al-thiqa'.
3
Assess supporting chains
If there are follow-up chains (mutaba'ah) or corroborating evidence, the hadith is strengthened.
Al-Layth ibn Sa'd had multiple chains supporting him.
4
Identify when addition leads to irregularity
If the narrator is not precise and the chains are contradictory without resolution, the hadith becomes mudtarib (irregular) and weak.
Irregularity occurs when the common link lacks precision.
5
Apply the example
Analyze the hadith about flogging: the added narrator (Jabir) is trustworthy, so the addition is accepted; the hadith remains authentic.
Al-Bukhari authenticated the shorter chain; the addition does not impair it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس: زيادة رجل في أحد الأسانيد إن من الشروط الأساسية لصحة الحديث الضبط، والزيادة والنقصان في سند من الأسانيد مع اتحاد المدار أمارة من أمارات عدم الضبط، وعدم الضبط مخرج للحديث من حال الصحة إلى حال الضعف. وعليه فإذا روي حديث بأسانيد متعددة، وكان مدار الحديث على رجل واحد، وزيد في أحد الأسانيد رجل ونقص من بقية الأسانيد، ولم نستطع الترجيح بين الروايات؛ مما يدل على أن الخطأ من الذي دار عليه الإسناد فرواه مرة هكذا، ومرة هكذا، فتبين لنا أن هذا الراوي لم يضبط هذا الحديث، فيحكم على الحديث بالاضطراب، ويتوقف الاحتجاج به حتى نجد له ما يعضده من متابعات، أو شواهد ترفعه من حال الضعف إلى حال القبول. وأحياناً توجد زيادة رجل في أحد الأسانيد، إلا أن الزيادة لا تقدح عند الأئمة إذا كان المزيد ثقة؛ لأن الإسناد كيفما دار دار على ثقة. وقد تختلف أنظار المحدثين في نحو مثل هذا فبعضهم يعد الزيادة قادحة وبعضهم لا يعدها قادحة. ومما وردت فيه زيادة واختلفت أنظار المحدّثين فيها، والراجح عدم القدح ما رواه بكير بن عبد الله، عن سليمان بن يسار، عن عبد الرَّحْمن بن جابر ابن عبد الله، عن أبي بردة، قال: كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «لا يجلد فوق عشر جلدات إلا في حد من حدود الله». فهذا الحديث مداره على بكير بن عبد الله، وهو هكذا من غير زيادة في إسناده وقد صححه من هذا الوجه الإمام البخاري، والترمذي. ورواه الليث بن سعد وهو ثقة ثبت، عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن بكير بن عبد الله. وتابعه سعيد بن أبي أيوب، وهو ثقة ثبت، فهذه متابعة تامة لليث بن سعد. وتابعه عبد الله بن لهيعة متابعة نازلة فرواه عن بكير بن عبد الله، به لكن قد خولف الإمام الليث بن سعد. خالفه زید بن أبي أنسية - وهو ثقة - عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن بكير بن عبد الله عن سليمان بن يسار، عن عبد الرَّحْمن بن جابر عن أبيه، عن أبي بردة بن نيار. تنبيه لليث بن سعد رواية أخرى في هذا الحديث فقد رواه عن بكير مباشرة فقد أخرجه الإمام أحمد ٤٦٦/٣ ، حدثنا سلمة الخزاعي قال حدثنا ليث عن بكير بن عبد الله ... الحديث، ثم قال سلمة الخزاعي: وكان ليث حدثناه ببغداد عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن بكير، عن سليمان، فلما كان بمصر قال أخبرناه بكير بن عبد الله بن الأشج.
The fifth type: Addition of a person in one of the chains. One of the basic conditions for the authenticity of a hadith is precision, and addition and omission in a chain among multiple chains while the pivot [of the chain] is the same is an indicator of lack of precision, and lack of precision removes the hadith from the state of authenticity to the state of weakness. Accordingly, if a hadith is narrated through multiple chains, and the pivot of the hadith is one person, and a person is added in one chain and omitted from the rest of the chains, and we are unable to give preponderance among the narrations, then this indicates that the error is from the one on whom the chain pivots, so he narrated it once this way and once that way, thus it becomes clear to us that this narrator did not master this hadith, so the hadith is judged to be irregular, and using it as evidence is suspended until we find what supports it from follow-up chains or corroborating evidences that raise it from the state of weakness to acceptability. Sometimes an addition of a person occurs in one chain, but the addition does not impair [the hadith] according to the imams if the added person is trustworthy, because the chain, however it turns, revolves around a trustworthy person. The views of hadith scholars may differ regarding such a case: some consider the addition impairing and some do not. Among what has been reported with an addition and the views of hadith scholars differed, and the preponderant view is that it is not impairing, is what was narrated by Bukayr ibn Abdullah, from Sulayman ibn Yasar, from Abd al-Rahman ibn Jabir ibn Abdullah, from Abi Barzah, who said: The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to say: 'No one should be flogged more than ten lashes except in a prescribed punishment of Allah.' This hadith's pivot is Bukayr ibn Abdullah, and it is like this without addition in its chain, and Imam al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi authenticated it from this aspect. Al-Layth ibn Sa'd, who is trustworthy and reliable, narrated it from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Bukayr ibn Abdullah. Sa'id ibn Abi Ayyub, who is trustworthy and reliable, followed him, so this is a complete follow-up for al-Layth ibn Sa'd. Abdullah ibn Lahi'ah followed him with a lower follow-up and narrated it from Bukayr ibn Abdullah, but Imam al-Layth ibn Sa'd was opposed. Opposing him was Zayd ibn Abi Unaysah, who is trustworthy, from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Bukayr ibn Abdullah, from Sulayman ibn Yasar, from Abd al-Rahman ibn Jabir from his father, from Abi Barzah ibn Niyar. Note: Al-Layth ibn Sa'd has another narration of this hadith, as he narrated it directly from Bukayr: Imam Ahmad 3/466 narrated it: Salamah al-Khuza'i told us: Layth told us from Bukayr ibn Abdullah... the hadith. Then Salamah al-Khuza'i said: Layth narrated it to us in Baghdad from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Bukayr, from Sulayman; then when he was in Egypt, he said: Bukayr ibn Abdullah ibn al-Ashaj told us.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth type of discrepancy in hadith chains: the addition of a narrator in one chain while other chains lack that addition. The author explains that such an addition, when the pivot of the chain is the same, is a sign of poor memory (lack of precision) in the narrator who is the common link. If the narrator is unreliable, the hadith becomes irregular (mudtarib) and cannot be used as evidence unless there are supporting narrations. However, if the added narrator is trustworthy, many hadith scholars do not consider the addition as a defect, because the chain then revolves around a reliable person. The example given is a hadith about flogging: 'No one should be flogged more than ten lashes except in a prescribed punishment of Allah.' The hadith's main chain is from Bukayr ibn Abdullah without addition. Some narrators added an extra person (Jabir ibn Abdullah) in the chain. The author notes the difference among scholars: some accept the addition (like al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi who authenticated the shorter chain), but others consider it problematic. The preferred view (al-rajih) is that the addition does not impair the hadith if the added narrator is reliable. The note (tanbih) provides an additional nuance: al-Layth ibn Sa'd himself narrated the hadith in two ways—once directly from Bukayr and once through an intermediary—indicating the complexity of such chains.

Key Terms
زيادة رجل (ziyādat rajul): The addition of a narrator in one chain of a hadith that is not present in other chains, which can indicate lack of precision or be acceptable if the added narrator is trustworthy.
متابعة (mutāba‘ah): A follow-up chain that supports the main chain by narrating the same hadith from the same narrator or from the same Companion, strengthening the hadith.
اضطراب (iḍṭirāb): Irregularity in a hadith due to contradictory narrations that cannot be reconciled, often resulting from lack of precision in a narrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does the addition of a narrator in one chain not impair the hadith?
A) If the addition is found in a single chain only
B) If the added narrator is trustworthy (thiqah)
C) If the hadith is from al-Bukhari and Muslim
D) If the pivot of the chain is unknown

Key Takeaway
The addition of a trustworthy narrator in a hadith chain does not impair the hadith; the Hanafi principle of ziyadat al-thiqa maqbula allows its acceptance.

The Impact of Chain Variation on Hadith Authenticity and Legal Rulings
Examines a hadith about a prescribed punishment with disputed chain of transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a hadith with conflicting chains, identify the central narrator (madār), evaluate follow-ups (mutāba‘āt), and explain how chain variation leads to a hadith being classified as mudtarib, affecting its use as evidence in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
When a hadith's chain varies irreconcilably, it becomes mudtarib and is not used as evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson explores a case study where a hadith about a prescribed punishment (hadd) has two conflicting chains: one without an extra narrator and one with an extra link. By tracing the chains and the scholars' evaluations, you will see how such variation can lead to disagreement on authenticity and ultimately affect whether the hadith is accepted as a legal proof. Understanding this process is crucial for applying Hanafi usul principles when assessing a ḥadīth's evidentiary value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Pivot (Madār)
Locate the central narrator around whom all chains revolve. The text states that the hadith's pivot is Bukayr ibn Abdullah. All chains go through him.
The madār is the key figure for tracing variations.
2
Trace the Two Main Chains
Follow the chain without addition (al-Layth → Yazid → Bukayr) and the chain with addition (Zayd → Yazid → Bukayr → Sulayman → Abd al-Rahman → his father → Abu Burda). Note the extra narrator 'his father' inserted between Abd al-Rahman and Abu Burda.
The addition creates a conflict between the transmissions.
3
Evaluate the Follow-Ups (Mutāba‘āt)
Identify which narrators support each version. Sa'id ibn Abi Ayyub provides a complete follow-up for al-Layth's version. For Zayd's version, Amr ibn al-Harith and Usama ibn Zayd give lower follow-ups, though Usama is weak. This confirms that the addition is not isolated.
A mutāba‘ah strengthens a chain but does not automatically resolve contradictions.
4
Assess the Scholars' Positions
List which scholars authenticated each version. Al-Bukhari and Muslim accepted the version with addition; al-Tirmidhi accepted the version without; al-Asili considered the hadith mudtarib due to irreconcilable differences. Understand that disagreement among hadith experts is a legitimate ikhtilāf.
The variation leads to different rulings on the hadith's strength.
5
Apply the Hanafi Usul Ruling on Mudtarib
According to Hanafi usul, a mudtarib hadith is weak and cannot be used as evidence. Therefore, if a hadith is deemed mudtarib, the Hanafi school relies on other sources (Quran, mutawatir sunnah, or qiyas) for the relevant ruling.
This decision impacts the legal ruling on the prescribed punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثَّالِث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن
جلدات إلا في حد من حدود الله».
فهذا الحديث مداره على بكير بن عبد الله ، وهو هكذا من غير زيادة في إسناده وقد صححه من هذا الوجه الإمام البخاري)، والترمذي). ورواه الليث بن سعد وهو ثقة ثبت ، عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن بكير بن عبد الله. وتابعه سعيد بن أبي أيوب، وهو ثقة ثبت، فهذه متابعة تامة لليث بن سعد.
وتابعه عبد الله بن لهيعة متابعة نازلة فرواه عن بكير بن عبد الله، به لكن قَدْ خولف الإمام الليث بن سعد. خالفه زید بن أبي أنيسة - وهو ثقة - عن يزيد بن أبي حبيب، عن بكير بن عبد الله عن سليمان بن يسار، عن عبد الرَّحْمن بن جابر عن أبيه، عن أبي بردة بن إلى آخر الحديث، فقد زاد زيد بن أبي أنيسة زيادة فأدخل جابـر بـن عـبـد الله بـيـن عبد الرحمن وأبي بردة.
وقد توبع زيد بن أبي أنيسة على هذا متابعة نازلة، تابعه اثنان:
الأول : عمرو بن الحارث، وهو ثقة فقيه حافظ.
الثاني: أسامة بن زيد، وهو صدوق يهم.
فروياه عن بكير بن عبد الله عن سليمان بن يسار عن عبد الرحمن بن جابر، عن أبيه، عن أبي بردة. هكذا روياه بزيادة: «أبيه» بين عبد الرحمن وأبي بردة فتابعا زيد بن أبي أنيسة.
هكذا حصلت الزيادة في أحد أسانيد الحديث، ومداره على راو واحد. وقد اختلفت وجهات نظر المحدثين:
فقد صحح الرواية بدون الزيادة الترمذي - كما سبق -، والدارقطني في العلل، – والبخاري:
وصحح الرواية مع الزيادة البخاري - أيضاً - ومسلم وأبو حاتم، والدارقطني في التتبع. وقد حكم باضطراب الحديث الأصيلي قال الحافظ: «ادعى الأصيلي أن الحديث مضطرب فلا يحتج به لاضطرابه».وَقَالَ الشوكاني: تكلم في إسناده ابن المنذر والأصيلي من جهة الاختلاف فيه.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. ...except flogging in one of God's prescribed punishments. This hadith's pivot is Bukayr ibn Abdullah, and it is like this without any addition in its chain. Al-Bukhari and al-Tirmidhi declared it authentic from this perspective. Al-Layth ibn Sa'd, who is trustworthy and firm, narrated it from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Bukayr ibn Abdullah. Sa'id ibn Abi Ayyub, who is trustworthy and firm, followed him; this is a complete follow-up to al-Layth ibn Sa'd. Abdullah ibn Lahī'a followed him with a lower follow-up, narrating it from Bukayr ibn Abdullah, but al-Layth ibn Sa'd was opposed. Zayd ibn Abi Unaysa, who is trustworthy, opposed him: he narrated from Yazid ibn Abi Habib, from Bukayr ibn Abdullah, from Sulayman ibn Yasar, from Abd al-Rahman ibn Jabir, from his father, from Abu Burda ibn... to the end of the hadith. Zayd ibn Abi Unaysa added an addition, inserting Jabir ibn Abdullah between Abd al-Rahman and Abu Burda. He was followed in this by two lower follow-ups: first, Amr ibn al-Harith, who is trustworthy, a jurist, and a memorizer; second, Usama ibn Zayd, who is truthful but makes mistakes. They narrated it from Bukayr ibn Abdullah, from Sulayman ibn Yasar, from Abd al-Rahman ibn Jabir, from his father, from Abu Burda. Thus they narrated with the addition of 'his father' between Abd al-Rahman and Abu Burda, so they followed Zayd ibn Abi Unaysa. Thus the addition occurred in one of the chains of the hadith, and its pivot is a single narrator. The views of the hadith scholars differed: At-Tirmidhi, ad-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal, and al-Bukhari authenticated the version without the addition. Al-Bukhari also, Muslim, Abu Hatim, and ad-Daraqutni in at-Tatabbu' authenticated the version with the addition. Al-Asili declared the hadith to be mudtarib; al-Hafiz said: 'Al-Asili claimed that the hadith is mudtarib, so it cannot be used as evidence due to its confusion.' Ash-Shawkani said: 'Ibn al-Mundhir and al-Asili criticized its chain due to the difference in it.'
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a case study in how differences in the chain of transmission (isnad) of a hadith can lead to disagreement among scholars regarding its authenticity. The hadith in question concerns a prescribed punishment (hadd), and its central narrator is Bukayr ibn Abdullah. The text traces two main chains: one without an extra link (from Bukayr via al-Layth) and one with an extra link (adding Jabir ibn Abdullah between Abd al-Rahman and Abu Burda). The scholars of hadith differ: some, like al-Bukhari and Muslim, accept the version with the addition as sahih; others, like at-Tirmidhi, accept the version without; and al-Asili considers the hadith mudtarib (confused) due to the irreconcilable variation. This disagreement directly impacts fiqh, as the legal ruling derived from this hadith—likely a matter of hudud—depends on whether the hadith is accepted as authentic. The Hanafi school typically acts on a hadith that is sahih or hasan, but if a hadith is deemed mudtarib, it may not be used as evidence. Thus, the variation in the chain can lead to different fiqh positions.

Key Terms
مدار (madār): The central narrator around whom the chains revolve; the pivot of the hadith.
متابعة (mutāba‘ah): A supporting chain that shares the same narrator from the teacher upward, confirming the hadith.
اضطراب (iḍṭirāb): A defect in a hadith where it is narrated in incompatible ways, rendering it weak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Asili declare the hadith to be mudtarib?
A) Because it was narrated only by weak narrators.
B) Because the different chains cannot be reconciled due to the addition of an extra narrator.
C) Because the hadith contradicts the Quran.
D) Because the hadith does not have a complete chain.

Key Takeaway
Chain variation that cannot be reconciled renders a hadith mudtarib and thus not a valid source for rulings in Hanafi usul.

Evaluating Chain Additions: The Ta'zir Hadith Dispute
Analyzing a hadith dispute about an addition in the chain affecting fiqh ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a variation in a hadith chain constitutes damaging idtirab or acceptable ziyadat al-thiqah, applying the Hanafi usul principle that accepts additions from reliable narrators.

Core Idea
A hadith chain variation is non-damaging if all routes trace back to a trustworthy narrator.

This passage examines a hadith about the number of lashes in ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The key issue is a disputed addition in the chain: Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa inserted an extra link between Abdurrahman ibn Jabir and Abu Burda. Scholars disagreed on whether this addition renders the hadith weak (mudtarib) or acceptable. By analyzing the views of hadith critics and Ibn Hajar's defense, you will learn how to assess such differences in the Hanafi usul framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the addition
Locate the extra narrator inserted by Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa. He added 'Jabir ibn Abdullah' between Abdurrahman and Abu Burda.
The addition is a 'ziyadat al-thiqah' from a narrator considered trustworthy.
2
Confirm the mutaba'ah
Check if trustworthy narrators supported Zaid. Two narrators (Amr ibn al-Harith and Usama ibn Zaid) followed Zaid with the same addition, albeit with a 'lower follow-up'. This strengthens the addition.
Mutaba'ah means a follow-up narration from a different chain, even if weaker.
3
Compare scholars' positions
List which scholars accepted or rejected the addition. For instance, Tirmidhi and Darqutni in al-Ilal accepted the version without addition; Bukhari, Muslim, and others accepted with addition.
This shows legitimate ikhtilaf among hadith masters, not necessarily a defect.
4
Evaluate the claim of idtirab
Understand Al-Asili's argument that the hadith is mudtarib (inconsistent). Then, analyze the author's refutation: the difference is non-damaging because all versions go back to trustworthy narrators.
Hanafi usul holds that idtirab is a defect only if the inconsistency cannot be resolved by preferring one reliable variant.
5
Apply Ibn Hajar's criterion
Ibn Hajar states: 'however it goes, it revolves around a trustworthy narrator.' This means as long as the core chain is sound, variations do not necessarily weaken the hadith. The two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) authenticated it.
This is the key principle: focus on the reliability of the narrators, not merely the existence of variations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْحَدِيث، فقد زاد زيد بن أبي أنيسة زيادة فأدخل جابر بن عبد الله بين عبد الرحمن وأبي بردة. وقد توبع زيد بن أبي أنيسة على هذا متابعة نازلة، تابعه اثنان: الأول: عمرو بن الحارث، وهو ثقة فقيه حافظ. الثاني: أسامة بن زيد، وهو صدوق يهم. فروياه عن بكير بن عبد الله عن سليمان بن يسار عن عبد الرحمن بن جابر، أبيه، عن أبي بردة. هكذا روياه بزيادة: «أبيه» بين عبد الرحمن وأبي بردة فتابعا زيد بن أبي أنيسة. هكذا حصلت الزيادة في أحد أسانيد الحديث، ومداره على راو واحد. وقد اختلفت وجهات نظر المحدثين: فقد صحح الرواية بدون الزيادة الترمذي - كما سبق -، والدارقطني في العلل، والبخاري: وصحح الرواية مع الزيادة البخاري - أيضاً - ومسلم وأبو حاتم، والدارقطني في التتبع. وقد حكم باضطراب الحديث الأصيلي قال الحافظ: «ادعى الأصيلي أن الحديث مضطرب فلا يحتج به لاضطرابه». وَقَالَ الشوكاني: تكلم في إسناده ابن المنذر والأصيلي من جهة الاختلاف فيه. ولم أجد النقل صريحاً عن ابن المنذر إلا أنه قال في الإشراف: لم نجد في عدد الضرب في التعزير خبراً عن رسول الله ثابتاً. أقول: ما ذكر من إعلال الحديث بالاضطراب هو أمرٌ غير صحيح؛ إذ إنَّهُ اختلاف غَيْر قادح فَهُوَ كيفما دار فَهُوَ عن ثقة، وَقَدْ دافع الحافظ ابن حجر عن هَذَا الْحَدِيث دفاعاً مجيداً، فَقَالَ: «لَمْ يقدح هَذَا الاختلاف عن الشيخين في صحة الْحَدِيث؛ فإنه كيفما دار يدور على ثقة ويحتمل أن يكون عبد الرَّحْمن وقع له فيه ما وقع لبكير بن الأشج في تحديث عبد الرَّحْمن بن جابر السليمان بحضرة بكير؛ ثم تحديث سليمان بكيراً به عن عبد الرَّحْمن، أو أن عبد الرَّحْمن سمع أبا بردة لما حدث به أباه، وثبته فيه أبوه، فحدّث به تارة بواسطة أبيه وتارة بغير واسطة وقد اتفق الشيخان على تصحيحه، وهما العمدة في التصحيح». وللحديث شواهد فقد أخرجه عبد الرزاق، والبخاري، والنسائي في الكبرى من طريق مسلم بن أبي مريم، عن عبد الرَّحْمن بن جابر، عمن سمع النبي ... الحديث. وقد أخرجه الحارث بن أبي أسامة، من رواية عبد الله بن أبي بكر بن الحارث بن هشام رفعه، وقوّى الحافظ ابن حجر سنده إلا أنه...
The hadith: Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa added an increase and inserted Jabir ibn Abdullah between Abdurrahman and Abu Burda. And Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa was followed in this with a lower follow-up; two followed him: first: Amr ibn al-Harith, who is trustworthy, a jurist, a memorizer. Second: Usama ibn Zaid, who is truthful but makes mistakes. So they narrated it from Bukair ibn Abdullah from Sulaiman ibn Yasar from Abdurrahman ibn Jabir, his father, from Abu Burda. Thus they narrated it with the addition: 'his father' between Abdurrahman and Abu Burda, so they followed Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa. Thus the addition occurred in one of the chains of the hadith, and its circulation is upon one narrator. And the views of the hadith scholars differed: At-Tirmidhi - as before - and Ad-Daraqutni in al-'Ilal authenticated the narration without the addition, and Al-Bukhari: and Al-Bukhari - also - Muslim, Abu Hatim, and Ad-Daraqutni in at-Ta'qib authenticated the narration with the addition. And Al-Asili ruled the hadith as disordered (mudtarib). Al-Hafiz said: 'Al-Asili claimed that the hadith is disordered, so it is not used as evidence due to its disorder.' And Ash-Shawkani said: Ibn al-Mundhir and Al-Asili criticized its chain due to the difference in it. And I did not find a clear transmission from Ibn al-Mundhir except that he said in al-Ishraf: We did not find regarding the number of lashes in ta'zir a report from the Messenger of Allah that is established. I say: What was mentioned of declaring the hadith defective due to disorder is incorrect; for it is a non-damaging difference, because however it goes, it goes back to a trustworthy narrator. And Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar defended this hadith magnificently, saying: 'This difference did not harm the authenticity of the hadith with the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim); for however it goes, it revolves around a trustworthy narrator. And it is possible that Abdurrahman experienced with it what Bukair ibn al-Ashaj experienced in narrating from Abdurrahman ibn Jabir as-Sulaymani in the presence of Bukair, then Sulayman narrated it to Bukair from Abdurrahman, or that Abdurrahman heard Abu Burda when he narrated it to his father, and his father confirmed it for him, so he narrated it sometimes through his father and sometimes without an intermediary. And the two shaykhs agreed on its authenticity, and they are the authorities in authentication.' And the hadith has supporting witnesses: Abdurrazzaq, Bukhari, and An-Nasa'i in al-Kubra narrated it via the route of Muslim ibn Abi Maryam, from Abdurrahman ibn Jabir, from whoever heard the Prophet...the hadith. And Al-Harith ibn Abi Usama narrated it, from the narration of Abdullah ibn Abi Bakr ibn al-Harith ibn Hisham, raising it, and Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar strengthened its chain except that...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of a hadith regarding the number of lashes in ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The core issue is a difference in the chain of narration: Zaid ibn Abi Unaisa added an extra link (Jabir ibn Abdullah) between Abdurrahman ibn Jabir and Abu Burda. This addition was followed by two other narrators. Hadith scholars disagreed: some like Tirmidhi and Darqutni accepted the version without the addition, while Bukhari, Muslim, and others accepted the version with it. Al-Asili claimed the hadith is mudtarib (inconsistent) and unreliable. The author refutes this, arguing that since all versions go back to trustworthy narrators, the difference is not damaging. Ibn Hajar's defense is quoted: the difference does not affect authenticity because it all traces to reliable narrators, and the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) authenticated it. Supporting narrations (shawahid) are cited. The author concludes that the claim of idtirab is incorrect.

Key Terms
زيادة الثقة (ziyadat al-thiqah): An additional word or phrase in a hadith added by a trustworthy narrator, which may be accepted or rejected depending on context.
متابعة (mutaba'ah): A follow-up narration supporting the original narrator, especially with lower strength.
اضطراب (idtirab): Inconsistency or disorder in a hadith, making it weak due to irreconcilable differences.
التعزير (at-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for sins for which no fixed hadd is prescribed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it all goes back to a trustworthy narrator.
C) Because the hadith is recorded in the Quran.
D) Because the addition was unanimously rejected.

Key Takeaway
A variation in a hadith chain is non-damaging if all versions trace back to trustworthy narrators, as defended by Ibn Hajar and accepted by Bukhari and Muslim.

Multiple Chains for One Hadith Text
Presentation of a hadith with multiple transmission chains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify variations in the chain (sanad) of a hadith and explain their significance for hadith reliability and legal derivation.

Core Idea
A single hadith text (matn) may be transmitted through multiple chains (sanad); differences in sanad affect authenticity and legal interpretation.

This lesson examines the famous hadith of the man who prayed incorrectly — known as Hadith al-Musī' al-Ṣalāt. You will see how scholars have transmitted this same matn through several chains, with slight differences in the narrator linkage. Understanding these variations is essential for evaluating the strength of a hadith and for grasping how ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) can arise from sanad issues in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith text (matn)
Read the Arabic matn carefully. It contains the Prophet's instructions on the essential acts of prayer (qibla, takbir, recitation of Fatiha, bowing, prostration, etc.). This matn is the common core transmitted in all chains.
2
Identify the main narrators in the chain
The source lists many narrators who transmitted the hadith via the chain of Ali ibn Yahya from his father from his uncle Rifa'ah (or from Rifa'ah directly). Note the names: al-Shafi'i, Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, etc.
The chain is the same for most, but with a key variation in the relationship between Yahya and Rifa'ah.
3
Spot the sanad variation
Compare al-Bukhari's chain (Ali from his father from Rifa'ah) with Abu Dawud's chain (Ali from his father from his uncle Rifa'ah). The difference: is Rifa'ah the father's brother (uncle) or just a relative? This affects the continuity and identity of the narrator.
Such variations can lead to different assessments of the hadith's authenticity.
4
Understand the usul implication
In Hanafi usul, a hadith with multiple authentic chains (mutaba'at) is stronger than one with a single chain. But variations in the chain may indicate a hidden weakness (e.g., an unknown narrator). This example shows why scholars check both sanad and matn.
The matn remains the same, but the chain differences require careful hadith criticism.
5
Link to fiqh derivation
The matn itself contains explicit fiqh rulings on prayer. Hanafis use this hadith to derive that reciting Surah al-Fatiha is obligatory (wajib) and that proper posture is required. The sanad variations do not change the ruling, but they affect the overall reliability of the proof.
In usul al-fiqh, the strength of the dalil determines whether the ruling is considered qat'i or zanni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النموذج الثاني حديث رفاعة بن رافع الزرقي، قال: جاء رجل ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في المسجد، فصلى قريباً منه، ثم انصرف إليه، فسلّم عليه، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أعد صلاتك، فإنك لم تصل» قال: فرجع فصلى نحواً مما صلى ثم انصرف إلى رسول الله، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أعد صلاتك فإنك لم تصل». فقال: يا رسول الله، كيف أصنع؟ فقال: «إذا استقبلت القبلة، فكبر، ثم اقرأ بأم القرآن، ثم اقرأ بما شئت، فإذا ركعت، فاجعل راحتيك على ركبتيك، وامدد ظهرك، فإذا رفعت رأسك، فأقم صلبك حتى ترجع العظام إلى مفاصلها، فإذا سجدت، فمكن سجودك، فإذا رفعت رأسك، فاجلس على فخذك اليسرى، ثم اصنع ذلك في كل ركعة». هذا الحديث أخرجه الشافعِي، وعبد الرزاق، وأحمد، والدارمي والبخاري، وأبو داود، وابن ماجه، والنسائي، وابن الجارود، والطحاوي وابن حبان، والطبراني، والدارقطني، والحاكم، والبيهقي، وابن حزم من طريق علي بن يحيى بن خلاد عن أبيه، عن عمه رفاعة بن رافع، فذكره. وأخرجه الطيالسي، وأبو داود، والترمذي، والنسائي، وابن ماجه، وغيرهم. واختلف في السند: فرواه البخاري عن علي بن يحيى عن أبيه عن رفاعة، ورواه أبو داود عن علي بن يحيى عن أبيه عن عمه رفاعة. وقد اختلف في كون رفاعة عم يحيى أم لا.
The second model: The hadith of Rifa'ah ibn Rafi' al-Zuraqi, he said: A man came while the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was in the mosque, and he prayed near him, then he turned to him, greeted him, and the Messenger of Allah said to him: 'Repeat your prayer, for you have not prayed.' He said: He returned and prayed similarly to what he had prayed, then he turned to the Messenger of Allah, and the Messenger of Allah said: 'Repeat your prayer, for you have not prayed.' He said: O Messenger of Allah, how should I do? He said: 'When you face the qibla, say takbir, then recite Umm al-Qur'an, then recite what you wish. When you bow, place your palms on your knees and straighten your back. When you raise your head, straighten your spine until the bones return to their joints. When you prostrate, make your prostration proper. When you raise your head, sit on your left thigh. Then do that in every rak'ah.' This hadith was narrated by al-Shafi'i, Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Darimi, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i, Ibn al-Jarud, al-Tahawi, Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn Hazm through the chain of Ali ibn Yahya ibn Khallad from his father, from his uncle Rifa'ah ibn Rafi', and he mentioned it. And it was narrated by al-Tayalisi, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and others. And there is disagreement in the chain: al-Bukhari narrated it from Ali ibn Yahya from his father from Rifa'ah, and Abu Dawud narrated it from Ali ibn Yahya from his father from his uncle Rifa'ah. And there is disagreement about whether Rifa'ah is the uncle of Yahya or not.

Commentary
This passage introduces the hadith of the man who prayed incorrectly, known as 'Hadith al-Musī' al-Ṣalāt.' The Prophet instructed him on the essential components of prayer. The text then lists a large number of hadith scholars who narrated this hadith through the chain of Ali ibn Yahya ibn Khallad from his father from his uncle Rifa'ah (or from Rifa'ah directly). This serves as an example of how a single hadith text (matn) can be transmitted through many chains (sanad), with slight variations in the chain (e.g., some narrate 'from his father from Rifa'ah' while others 'from his father from his uncle Rifa'ah'). Such differences in sanad can affect the strength of the hadith or lead to different fiqh rulings. In usul al-fiqh, this is a classic illustration of ikhtilaf fi al-sanad and its impact on the reliability and interpretation of the text. The key point is that despite multiple chains, the matn remains consistent, underscoring the importance of both sanad and matn in hadith criticism.

Key Terms
سند (sanad): The chain of narrators transmitting the hadith.
متن (matn): The text/content of the hadith.
اختلاف السند (ikhtilaf al-sanad): Variation in the chain of narrators for the same hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A single hadith text (matn) can be transmitted through many chains (sanad), and careful analysis of sanad variations is crucial for determining the hadith's reliability and its application in fiqh derivation.

The Hadith of Rifa'a bin Rafi': Analyzing Chain Differences
Analysis of multiple chains of the prayer hadith of Rifa'a bin Rafi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a hadith with multiple chains and explain why chain inconsistency does not always invalidate the hadith according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
A hadith with inconsistent chains (idtirab) remains authentic if corroborated by another reliable hadith.

This passage presents a famous hadith on prayer description with multiple chains that differ in the names of narrators. The author shows that despite idtirab, the hadith is considered authentic due to supporting evidence. We will examine how Hanafi usul treats such textual variations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn and its content
Read the hadith text provided at the beginning. It describes the actions of prayer: facing qibla, takbir, reciting Fatiha, bowing, raising head, prostrating, sitting, etc.
The matn is a detailed description of the Prophet's prayer.
2
List the different chains cited
The author lists numerous narrators and chains. Note the variations: some through 'Ali ibn Yahya from his father from his uncle Rifa'a, others through Yahya ibn Ali from his father from his grandfather Rifa'a, and some directly from 'Ali ibn Yahya from Rifa'a.
The chains differ in the number of intermediaries and names.
3
Recognize the specific differences (idtirab)
Observe that in some narrations an extra link (father or grandfather) is added or omitted. This inconsistency in the sanad is called idtirab (اضطرب).
idtirab is a technical term in hadith criticism indicating irregularity.
4
Understand the impact of idtirab on authenticity
The author notes that despite this idtirab, no scholar declared the hadith weak. This is because the hadith is supported by the authentic hadith of Abu Hurayrah on the same topic, and Imam al-Nawawi authenticated it.
In Hanafi usul, chain differences alone do not invalidate a hadith if there is corroboration.
5
Apply the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi approach relies on the overall strength of the evidence. Here, the multiplicity of chains and the support from another authentic narration outweigh the idtirab.
This demonstrates that textual analysis requires holistic evaluation.
