⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن
إذا استقبلت القبلة، فكبر، ثم اقرأ بأم القرآن، ثم اقرأ بما شئت، فإذا ركعت، فاجعل راحتيك على ركبتيك، وامدد ظهرك، فإذا رفعت رأسك، فأقم صلبك حتى ترجع العظام إلى مفاصلها، فإذا سجدت، فمكن سجودك، فإذا رفعت رأسك، فاجلس على فخذك اليسرى، ثم اصنع ذلك في كل ركعة. هذا الحديث أخرجه الشافعي وعبد الرزاق وأحمد والدارمي والبخاري وأبو داود وابن ماجه والنسائي وابن الجارود والطحاوي وابن حبان والطبراني والدارقطني والحاكم والبيهقي وابن حزم من طريق علي بن يحيى بن خلاد عن أبيه عن عمه رفاعة بن رافع فذكره. وأخرجه الطيالسي وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وابن خزيمة والطحاوي والطبراني في الكبير والبيهقي والبغوي من طريق يحيى بن علي بن يحيى بن خلاد عن أبيه عن جده عن رفاعة بن رافع فذكره. وأخرجه الطحاوي من طريق يحيى بن علي بن يحيى بن خلاد عن أبيه عن جده رفاعة بن رافع فذكره. وأخرجه الشافعي وأحمد والبخاري وأبو داود والطحاوي والطبراني من طريق علي بن يحيى عن رفاعة بن رافع فذكره. في بعض الروايات عن علي عن عمه وفي بعضها عن علي عن رفاعة وفي بعضها عن علي عن عمه رفاعة. هكذا اضطرب في هذا الحديث وزيد في إسناده وقد نوه على الاختلاف الطحاوي في شرح مشكل الآثار إلا أن هذا الحديث لم يقدح بصحته أحد – فيما أعلم – لصحة حديث أبي هريرة على أن الإمام النووي صحح حديث رفاعة فقال حديث رفاعة
Chapter Three: Difference in Sanad and Matn. 'When you face the qibla, say the takbir, then recite Umm al-Qur'an, then recite what you wish. When you bow, place your palms on your knees and straighten your back. When you raise your head, straighten your spine until the bones return to their joints. When you prostrate, prostrate fully. When you raise your head, sit on your left thigh, then do that in every rak'ah.' This hadith was narrated by al-Shafi'i, 'Abd al-Razzaq, Ahmad, al-Darimi, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i, Ibn al-Jarud, al-Tahawi, Ibn Hibban, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim, al-Bayhaqi, and Ibn Hazm via 'Ali ibn Yahya ibn Khallad from his father, from his uncle Rifa'a ibn Rafi'. And it was narrated by al-Tayalisi, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Khuzaymah, al-Tahawi, al-Tabarani in al-Kabir, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Baghawi via Yahya ibn 'Ali ibn Yahya ibn Khallad from his father, from his grandfather, from Rifa'a ibn Rafi'. And it was narrated by al-Tahawi via Yahya ibn 'Ali ibn Yahya ibn Khallad from his father, from his grandfather Rifa'a ibn Rafi'. And it was narrated by al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, al-Tahawi, and al-Tabarani via 'Ali ibn Yahya from Rifa'a ibn Rafi'. In some narrations, 'from 'Ali from his uncle', in some 'from 'Ali from Rifa'a', and in some 'from 'Ali from his uncle Rifa'a'. Thus this hadith has inconsistency and addition in its chain, and al-Tahawi in Sharh Mushkil al-Athar pointed out the difference. However, no one – as far as I know – has impugned its authenticity due to the authenticity of the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, and al-Nawawi authenticated the hadith of Rifa'a, saying: 'Hadith Rifa'a...'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a well-known hadith about the description of the Prophet's prayer, narrated by Rifa'a ibn Rafi'. The author, in a chapter titled 'Difference in Sanad and Matn', first provides the full matn (text) of the hadith, which includes instructions on facing the qibla, takbir, recitation of Fatiha, bowing, raising the head, prostration, and sitting. Then the author lists numerous chains of narration (asaneed) for this hadith, showing the multiplicity of narrators and the variations in the names of the narrators (e.g., 'Ali ibn Yahya from his father from his uncle Rifa'a' vs. 'Yahya ibn 'Ali from his father from his grandfather Rifa'a', etc.). The author highlights the inconsistency (idtirab) in the chain, noting that some narrations add an extra link (father or grandfather) while others skip it. Despite these differences, the author asserts that no scholar has declared the hadith weak, citing the authentic hadith of Abu Hurayrah on the same topic and the endorsement of Imam al-Nawawi, who authenticated Rifa'a's hadith. This demonstrates that variations in the chain do not automatically invalidate a hadith, especially when supporting evidence exists.

Key Terms
اضطرب (idtaraaba): In this context, it refers to inconsistency or fluctuation in the chain of narration of a hadith, where narrators differ in reporting the name or lineage of intermediate narrators.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the term 'اضطرب' (idtirab) in the context of this hadith refer to?
A) The hadith is weak and cannot be used as evidence.
B) There is inconsistency in the chain of narration among different transmitters.
C) The text of the hadith contradicts the Qur'an.
D) The hadith is fabricated and has no basis.

Key Takeaway
Chain inconsistency (idtirab) does not automatically weaken a hadith; authentication depends on overall corroboration and the strength of supporting narrations.

Hadith Transmissions and Obligation of Tamkin: A Usul Analysis
Examines hadith variants and uses them to support the obligation of tranquility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) to derive the ruling of tamkin in prayer from the hadith of the one who prayed badly, and evaluate how transmission differences affect the istinbat process.

Core Idea
The Prophet's command to be tranquil in prayer indicates obligation – the amr is for wujub unless a qarina overrides.

This lesson explores how differences in hadith transmission (ikhtilaf fi al-sanad wa al-matn) impact the derivation of fiqh rulings. We focus on the hadith of the one who prayed badly (hadith al-musi' fi salatihi) and its use to establish the obligation of tranquility (tamkin) in ruku' and sujud. The passage highlights that despite chain variations, the hadith remains authentic and provides a clear command (amr) that, according to Hanafi usul, indicates obligation. We will trace the istinbat chain and examine an internal Hanafi disagreement on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify Key Textual Elements
Identify the specific phrases in the hadith that serve as the basis for deriving the ruling. Focus on 'you have not prayed' (lam tusalli) and the commands 'bow until you are tranquil' (irka' hatta tatma'inna) and 'prostrate until you are tranquil' (usjud hatta tatma'inna).
These are explicit (mantuuq) statements from the Prophet.
2
Classify the Textual Category
Determine the usul category of these phrases. The commands are in the imperative form (amr), which is categorized as 'zahir' in Hanafi usul – it indicates obligation unless there is a qarina to the contrary.
The negative judgment 'you have not prayed' reinforces that the commanded acts are essential.
3
Apply the Usul Principle
Apply the Hanafi principle 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub' (the command indicates obligation). Since the Prophet commanded tamkin after declaring the prayer invalid without it, tamkin becomes obligatory (wajib/fard).
The absence of a qarina (contextual indicator) for recommendation means the default of obligation applies.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf and Its Cause
Recognize that despite this derivation, some Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) consider tamkin sunnah, not fard. Their reasoning is that the Prophet's own practice sometimes showed slight haste, acting as a qarina to reduce the command from obligation to recommendation.
This internal Hanafi difference stems from disagreement over the presence of a qarina that overrides the apparent meaning of the amr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن ... هكذا اضطرب في هذا الحديث وزيد في إسناده، وقد نوه على الاختلاف الطحاوي إلا أن هذا الحديث لم يقدح بصحته أحد – فيما أعلم – لصحته حديث أبي هريرة، على أن الإمام النووي صحح حديث رفاعة فقال: «حديث رفاعة صحيح، والطمأنينة واجبة في السجود عندنا وعند الجمهور». أثر هذا الحديث في اختلاف الفقهاء كم الطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود، وبين السجدتين، والاعتدال من الركوع) وما دمنا قد تكلمنا عن تخريج حديث رفاعة بإسهاب، فسأذكر ما له من أثر في اختلاف الفقهاء: الطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود الأول: اختلف الفقهاء رحمهم الله في هذه المسألة على قولين: الطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود فرض فمن تركها فصلاته باطلة. وهو قول الإمام سعيد بن المسيب، وإليه ذهب أحمد، والشافعي، وأبو يوسف، وهو وجه للمالكية. ودليلهم حديث رفاعة، وحديث أبي هريرة ولاسيما قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم للمسيء في صلاته: «ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل» ثم قوله بعد ذلك : اركع حتى تطمئن راكعاً، ثم ارفع حتى تعتدل قائماً، ثم اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجداً). ووجه الدلالة: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم جعل الصلاة الخالية من الطمأنينة كلا صلاة، ثم أمره بعد ذلك بالطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود، والأمر للوجوب.
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text ... Thus this hadith has been disturbed and added to its chain, and al-Tahawi noted the difference, but this hadith was not impugned by anyone – as far as I know – because of the soundness of the hadith of Abu Hurayra, and also Imam al-Nawawi authenticated the hadith of Rifa'a and said: 'The hadith of Rifa'a is sound, and tranquility is obligatory in prostration according to us and the majority.' ... The effect of this hadith in the difference of jurists: ... They differed on this issue in two opinions: Tranquility in bowing and prostration is obligatory; whoever abandons it, his prayer is invalid. This is the statement of Imam Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, and Ahmad, al-Shafi'i, Abu Yusuf, and it is a view among the Malikis. Their evidence is the hadith of Rifa'a and the hadith of Abu Hurayra, especially his statement to the one who prayed badly: 'Go back and pray, for you have not prayed' then his statement: 'Bow until you are tranquil in bowing, then raise until you stand straight, then prostrate until you are tranquil in prostration.' And the indication: The Prophet made the prayer devoid of tranquility as no prayer, then commanded him to have tranquility in bowing and prostration, and the command is for obligation.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a case study in the influence of hadith transmission differences on juristic disagreement. The author first discusses the various chains of the hadith of Rifa'a, noting that despite discrepancies, the hadith remains authentic due to corroborating evidence from Abu Hurayra. He quotes Imam al-Nawawi's authentication. Then he moves to the fiqh effect: the obligation of tamkin (tranquility) in ruku' and sujud. He presents the first of two opinions: tamkin is fard, rendering the prayer invalid if omitted. This view is held by Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, Ahmad, al-Shafi'i, and notably Abu Yusuf of the Hanafi school, indicating internal Hanafi difference. The evidence is the hadith of the one who prayed badly. The derivation (istinbat) is clear: the Prophet declared the prayer without tamkin as no prayer, and then commanded tamkin. Since commands (awamir) default to obligation, tamkin is fard. This passage illustrates how a hadith's authenticity is established despite chain variants, and how it is then applied to derive a ruling.

Key Terms
الطمأنينة (al-tamānīnah): In this context, it means tranquility and stillness in the prayer postures, such that each limb settles.
الأمر (al-amr): A command or directive from the Lawgiver, which by default indicates obligation according to many usulis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of tamkin (tranquility) in ruku' and sujud based on the hadith of the one who prayed badly?
A) It is sunnah.
B) It is obligatory (fard), and abandoning it invalidates the prayer.
C) It is recommended but not required.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
The explicit command to be tranquil in prayer indicates obligation, demonstrating how the usul principle al-amr yufidu al-wujub directly impacts fiqh rulings, though internal Hanafi ikhtilaf exists on the presence of a qarina.

Chain Discrepancies and Hadith Authenticity: The Zakat al-Fitr Case
Case study of a hadith chain discrepancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a chain discrepancy in a hadith and evaluate its impact on authentication using Hanafi usul principles.

Core Idea
Uncertainty in a narrator's name weakens a hadith, affecting the legal ruling derived from it.

This passage takes you into the workshop of a Hanafi hadith critic. You will see how even a single unclear narrator name can cause a hadith to be classified as weak, and how this impacts fiqh rulings like the amount of Zakat al-Fitr. The analysis demonstrates the meticulous care required in evaluating evidence before deriving legal rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its legal content
Locate the text of the Zakat al-Fitr hadith in the passage. Note the prescribed amounts (1 sa' of dates/barley or half sa' of wheat) and the categories of people obligated.
This hadith is a potential source for a fiqh ruling on the quantity of Zakat al-Fitr.
2
List the chain variants presented
From the chains cited (via 'Affan, Musaddad, etc.), extract the different names given for the narrator from al-Zuhri: Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, Tha'laba ibn Abdullah, Abdullah ibn Tha'laba, etc.
The variation centers on the identity of the narrator directly above al-Zuhri.
3
Analyze the discrepancies
Examine the nature of the variance: is it in the name, kunya, or nasab? Note the comments from Ibn Hazm (majhul al-hal) and al-Zayla'i (two defects). Consider whether the discrepancies point to a single confused narrator or two different individuals.
Hanafi usul treats uncertainty in narrator identity as a cause for weakness (da'f).
4
Evaluate the authenticity conclusion and fiqh implications
Based on the analysis, decide the status of the hadith (weak due to the chain discrepancy). Understand that this weakness means the hadith cannot be used as the sole basis for the Zakat al-Fitr ruling, affecting the fiqh position.
Other stronger evidence would be needed to establish the ruling.

الفصل الثَّالِث / الاختلاف في السند والمتن

مدارها واحداً، ومصدر خروجها واحداً، فإذا حصل الاختلاف على من هذا شأنه فهو أمر يهتم به العلماء غاية الاهتمام؛ إذ هو يدلل على خلل طارئ من الأصل الذي روى الحديث أو من الرواة عنه. فإذا توبع الرواة على اختلاف رواياتهم فالحمل إذن على من دارت عليه الأسانيد، فهو بلا شك حدث الجميع على أوجه مختلفة متباينة فهو إذن فاقد لضبط الحديث في هذا الحديث خاصة، وإن كان من الثقات الأثبات ومن أنواع تلك الاختلافات الكثيرة: الاختلاف في اسم الراوي ونسبه.

ومما اختلف الرواة فيه اختلافاً كبيراً ما رواه الطحاوي (١) من طريق عفان ومسدد، عن حماد بن زيد، عن النعمان بن راشد، عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن أبي صغير، عن أبيه، قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أدوا زكاة الفطر صاعاً من تمر، أو صاعاً من شعير، أو نصف صاع من بر – أو قال: قمح - عن كل إنسان صغير أو كبير، ذكر أو أنثى، حر أو مملوك، غني أو فقير». أقول هذا الحديث هو حديث شيخ الزهري ثعلبة بن أبي صعير – كما في وقد اختلف في اسمه ونسبه اختلافاً كثيراً حتى إن بعض أهل العلم
الرواية الآنفة
(-
ضعف الحديث به
قال ابن حزم هذا الحديث راجع إلى رجل مجهول الحال مضطرب عنه مختلف في اسمه مرة: عبد الله بن ثعلبة، ومرة: ثعلبة بن عبد الله، ولا خلاف في أن الزهري لم يلق ثعلبة بن أبي صغير، وليس لعبد الله بن ثعلبة صحبة»(۲).
وَقَالَ الزيلعي في " نصب الراية ": «حاصل ما يعلـل بـه هـذا الحديث أمران: أحدهما: الاختلاف في اسم أبي صغير، فقد تقدم من جهة أبي داود عن مسدد: ثعلبة بن أبي صغير، أو ثعلبة بن عبد الله بن أبي صغير، وكذلك أيضاً عن أبي داود في رواية بكر ابن وائل المتقدمة: ثعلبة بن عبد الله أو قال عبد الله بن ثعلبة على الشك، وعنده أيضاً من رواية محمد بن يحيى، وفيه الجزم بعبد الله بن ثعلبة بن أبي صغير، وكذلك رواية ابن جريج، وعند الدارقطني من رواية مسدد عن ابن أبي ضعير، عن أبيه لم يسمه ...)) (۳).
ولهذا الاختلاف الشديد مال الحافظ إلى التفريق وجعلهما اثنين فقال: «هذا
يقتضي أن يكون ثعلبة بن صغير غير ثعلبة بن أبي صغير، والله أعلم. وقد حاولت جاهداً جمع طرق الحديث والتنقيب عن الاختلافات الواردة فيه، وسأفصل ذلك، فأقول:
الحديث سبق ذكره من رواية النعمان بن راشد وعنه حماد بن زيد وقد اختلف على
هذا الطريق
النعمان
فقد أخرجه الإمام أحمد (۱) من طريق عفان بن مسلم عن حماد بن زيد، عن بن راشد عن الزهري عن ابن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه. وأخرجه البخاري عن مسدد عن الزهري، عن ثعلبة بن صعير، عن أبيه. وأخرجه أبو داود، عن سليمان بن داود عن حماد بن زيد عن النعمان بن داود، عن الزهري، عن ثعلبة بن عبد الله بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه. وأخرجه الفسوي، عن أبي النعمان عن حماد بن زيد، عن النعمان بن راشد، عن الزهري، عن عبد الله بن ثعلبة، عن أبيه. وأخرجه ابن قانع، قال: حدثنا الحسن بن المثنى، قال: حدثنا: عفان، قَالَ: حدثنا أحمد بن بشر المرتدي، قَالَ: حدثنا خالد بن خداش جميعاً، حماد بن زيد، عن النعمان بن راشد عن الزهري عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه.
Chapter Three: Differences in the Chain and Text. They have one source and one origin. When difference occurs regarding one like this, it is a matter of great concern to scholars; for it indicates a defect that arose from the original source that narrated the hadith or from the narrators about him. If the narrators are followed up on the differences of their narrations, then the blame lies on the one around whom the chains revolve. He undoubtedly narrated to all in different divergent ways, thus he is lacking in precision of hadith in this particular hadith, even if he is among the reliable and trustworthy. Among those many types of differences is the difference in the name of the narrator and his lineage. Among what the narrators differed over greatly is what al-Tahawi narrated via 'Affan and Musaddad, from Hammad ibn Zayd, from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid, from al-Zuhri, from Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, from his father, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Give Zakat al-Fitr: a sa' of dates, or a sa' of barley, or half a sa' of wheat – or he said: qamh – for every person, young or old, male or female, free or slave, rich or poor.' I say: This hadith is the hadith of the shaykh of al-Zuhri, Tha'laba ibn Abi Su'ayr – as in the preceding narration – and they differed in his name and lineage greatly, so much so that some scholars weakened the hadith because of it. Ibn Hazm said: 'This hadith goes back to a man of unknown status, confused about him, differing in his name: sometimes Abdullah ibn Tha'laba, sometimes Tha'laba ibn Abdullah, and there is no dispute that al-Zuhri did not meet Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, and Abdullah ibn Tha'laba had no companionship.' Al-Zayla'i said in Nasb al-Rayah: 'The sum of what this hadith is criticized for are two matters: first, the difference in the name of Abu Sughayr, as it was presented from Abu Dawud from Musaddad: Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, or Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Sughayr, and likewise from Abu Dawud in the narration of Bakr ibn Wail previously: Tha'laba ibn Abdullah or he said Abdullah ibn Tha'laba out of doubt, and also from the narration of Muhammad ibn Yahya, and in it the certainty of Abdullah ibn Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, and likewise the narration of Ibn Jurayj, and from al-Daraqutni from the narration of Musaddad from Ibn Abi Du'ayr, from his father, he did not name him...' Due to this severe difference, the Hafiz inclined to differentiate and make them two, saying: 'This requires that Tha'laba ibn Sughayr be other than Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, and Allah knows best. And I have tried hard to collect the chains of the hadith and search for the differences found in it, and I will detail that.' So I say: The hadith was previously mentioned from the narration of al-Nu'man ibn Rashid and from him Hammad ibn Zayd, and they differed on this path of al-Nu'man. Imam Ahmad narrated it via 'Affan ibn Muslim from Hammad ibn Zayd, from Ibn Rashid from al-Zuhri from Ibn Tha'laba ibn Abi Su'ayr, from his father. Al-Bukhari narrated it from Musaddad from al-Zuhri, from Tha'laba ibn Su'ayr, from his father. Abu Dawud narrated it from Sulayman ibn Dawud from Hammad ibn Zayd from al-Nu'man ibn Dawud, from al-Zuhri, from Tha'laba ibn Abdullah ibn Abi Su'ayr, from his father. Al-Fasawi narrated it from Abu al-Nu'man from Hammad ibn Zayd, from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid, from al-Zuhri, from Abdullah ibn Tha'laba, from his father. Ibn Qani' narrated it: said: narrated to us al-Hasan ibn al-Muthanna, said: narrated to us 'Affan, said: narrated to us Ahmad ibn Bishr al-Murtadi, said: narrated to us Khalid ibn Khidash all together, from Hammad ibn Zayd, from al-Nu'man ibn Rashid, from al-Zuhri, from Tha'laba ibn Abi Su'ayr, from his father.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a case study from usul al-hadith demonstrating how discrepancies in the chain (sanad) and text (matn) affect the authenticity of a hadith, and consequently juristic rulings. The chapter title indicates the focus on differences in sanad and matn. The author begins with a general principle: when narrators agree on the source but differ in transmission, it points to a defect. He then examines a specific hadith about Zakat al-Fitr (the charity at the end of Ramadan). The hadith prescribes one sa' of dates or barley, or half a sa' of wheat (or qamh) per person. The key issue is the variation in the name of the narrator from al-Zuhri: Tha'laba ibn Abi Sughayr, Tha'laba ibn Abdullah, Abdullah ibn Tha'laba, etc. The author quotes Ibn Hazm, who weakens the hadith because the narrator is 'majhul al-hal' (of unknown status) and his name is inconsistent. He also notes that al-Zuhri did not meet Tha'laba, implying a broken chain. Then he cites al-Zayla'i, who identifies two main defects: the confusion over the name of Abu Sughayr (the father) and the varying names in different chains. The author mentions that al-Hafiz (Ibn Hajar) considered there might be two different individuals. To resolve, the author collects all available chains and lists them, showing how the same hadith is transmitted with different names—Tha'laba ibn Abi Su'ayr, Tha'laba ibn Abdullah, etc. This multiplicity confirms the weakness due to uncertainty about the narrator's identity. The passage illustrates how hadith criticism interacts with fiqh: the authenticity of this hadith affects the ruling on the quantity of Zakat al-Fitr. The Hanafi school, while relying on such narrations when corroborated, here acknowledges the weakness. The author's method of collecting all variants and analyzing discrepancies is consistent with Hanafi usul, which emphasizes careful examination of narrations to determine legal rulings.

Key Terms
الاختلاف في السند والمتن (al-ikhtilaf fi al-sanad wa al-matn): Discrepancy occurring in the chain of transmission or the text of a hadith
الراوي (al-rawi): The narrator who transmits the hadith
الاسم والنسب (al-ism wa al-nasab): The name and lineage of a narrator, which when disputed affects reliability
مجهول الحال (majhul al-hal): A narrator of unknown status, whose reliability cannot be determined

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary defect identified in the Zakat al-Fitr hadith according to the passage?
A) The hadith is mursal because al-Zuhri did not hear it from Tha'laba
B) The narrator Tha'laba is of unknown status and his name varies across chains
C) The hadith contradicts the Quran
D) The hadith was only transmitted by weak narrators

Key Takeaway
A single discrepancy in a narrator's name can render a hadith weak, impacting fiqh rulings dependent on it.

The Second Opinion on Sadaqat al-Fitr: Evidence from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri
Second opinion on sadaqat al-fitr with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of relying on authentic ahadith to resolve ikhtilaf in sadaqat al-fitr rulings, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the second opinion and Ibn Hazm's view.

Core Idea
A single sa' of any grain suffices, supported by the broader hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri.

In this lesson, we examine the second major opinion on the quantity and type of grain for sadaqat al-fitr. The passage presents the view of many Companions and leading imams that one sa' of any grain is acceptable. We will analyze the key hadith evidence from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and understand how differences in hadith transmission lead to divergent rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opinion and its proponents
Locate the second opinion: 'one sa' of any grain' and note the list of Companions and imams who held it.
Proponents include Aisha, Ibn Umar, Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri as quoted. Identify the listed items and the key phrase 'sa' min ta'am'.
The hadith mentions wheat, barley, dates, raisins, and dried cheese.
3
Analyze the textual and transmission features
Assess the chain (Zayd ibn Aslam → Iyad → Abu Sa'id) and the explicit allowance of multiple grains.
The text shows a general term (ta'am) which some interpret broadly.
4

Ibn Hazm relies on a different hadith and a stricter approach to hadith criticism.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf

This passage presents the second opinion regarding the quantity and type of grain permissible for sadaqat al-fitr. The first opinion (implied) required only specific grains, but the second opinion, held by many companions and later imams like Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad, allows one sa' of any grain. The text then notes Ibn Hazm's strict opposing view, limiting it to dates or barley. The discussion illustrates how differences in hadith transmission and interpretation lead to divergent fiqh rulings.

Key Terms

صدقة الفطر (ṣadaqat al-fiṭr): The obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan before the Eid prayer.

Hadith Variants and Ruling Scope: Zakat al-Fitr
Textual analysis of hadith variants leading to fiqh dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hadith basis for the Hanafi position on Zakat al-Fitr and evaluate Ibn Hazm's critique of that basis using usul al-hadith principles.

Core Idea
A hadith's authenticity and its scope determine the range of permissible grains for Zakat al-Fitr.

This lesson examines how differences in hadith authentication and textual scope lead to a concrete fiqh dispute: which grains are acceptable for Zakat al-Fitr? The Hanafi school, following the majority, permits wheat and other grains based on a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri. Ibn Hazm (Zahiri) restricts the payment to only dates and barley, relying on the hadith of Ibn 'Umar and weakening the contrary evidence. Understanding this dispute trains you to see how a single usul principle—the evaluation of hadith and its general or specific application—shapes rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith cited by each side
Locate the two key hadiths in the passage: the hadith of Abu Sa'id (allowing wheat) and the hadith of Ibn 'Umar (restricting to dates/barley). Note that Ibn Hazm weakens the former and relies solely on the latter.
The hadith of Ibn 'Umar uses the word 'or' (أو), which is ambiguous between amm and khass.
2
Classify the textual scope of each hadith
Determine whether the wording in each hadith is general (amm) or specific (khass). The hadith of Ibn 'Umar mentions only two items; does that imply limitation (khass) or is it merely an example (amm)?
Hanafi usul: if a hadith lists examples, it may remain general unless a specific exclusion is proven.
3
Assess the authenticity claims
Ibn Hazm weakens the hadith of Ibn Abi Saghir (which supports Abu Sa'id's report). Understand that the dispute involves evaluating the chain (isnad) and text (matn). The Hanafis consider the hadith of Abu Sa'id authentic because it was acted upon by the Companions.
Companion practice (amal al-sahabah) is a source of tarjih in Hanafi usul.
4
Link text and authenticity to ruling scope
Given the Hanafi acceptance of Abu Sa'id's hadith and its general implication, the ruling allows any grain that substitutes for a sa' of dates. Ibn Hazm's rejection of that hadith restricts the ruling to the explicit items in Ibn 'Umar's hadith.
5
State the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: Hanafis and the majority permit wheat, raisins, etc.; Ibn Hazm's followers only dates or barley. This shows how usul choices directly affect fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سَمْرَاءِ الشَّامِ تَعْدِلُ صَاعاً من تَمْرِ. قال فأخذَ النَّاسُ بذلك. قال أبو سعيد: فلا أزالُ أخرجه كما كُنْتُ أخرجه (۱). وخالف ذلك كله ابن حزم - رحمه الله - فذهب إلى أنه لا يجزئ في زكاة الفطر إلا صاع من التمر أو الشعير، ولا يجزئ غيره). وحجته تضعيفه لحديث ابن أبي صغير واقتصاره على ما ورد في حديث ابن عمر: «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فرض زكاة الفطر من رمضان على الناس صاعاً من تمر، أو صاعاً من شعير، على كل حر أو عبد ذكر أو أنثى، من المسلمين).
The wheat of Syria is equivalent to a sa' of dates. He said: So the people adopted that. Abu Sa'id said: I will continue to give it as I used to give it. And Ibn Hazm - may Allah have mercy on him - opposed all of that, holding that nothing suffices for Zakat al-Fitr except a sa' of dates or barley, and nothing else suffices. His proof is his weakening of the hadith of Ibn Abi Saghir and his restriction to what is reported in the hadith of Ibn 'Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, made Zakat al-Fitr of Ramadan obligatory upon the people: a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley, upon every free person or slave, male or female, among the Muslims.'
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of a fiqh dispute arising from differences in hadith. The author begins by referencing a hadith where Abu Sa'id al-Khudri states that a sa' of Syrian wheat (samra' al-Sham) is equivalent to a sa' of dates for Zakat al-Fitr, and that people adopted this practice. Ibn Hazm (the Zahiri scholar) opposes this, arguing that only dates or barley are permissible. His evidence is his critique of the hadith that allowed other grains, and his reliance on the more restricted hadith of Ibn 'Umar, which mentions only dates and barley. The dispute illustrates how differing assessments of hadith authenticity and textual scope lead to divergent fiqh rulings. The Hanafi school, following the majority, accepts other grains like wheat based on the practice of the Companions and the hadith of Abu Sa'id.

Key Terms
زكاة الفطر (Zakat al-Fitr): An obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan.
سمراء الشام (Samra' al-Sham): A type of wheat from Syria.
صاع (sa'): A measure of volume, approximately 2.4 kg.

Differences in hadith authentication and textual scope directly produce divergent fiqh rulings, as seen in the permissibility of grains for Zakat al-Fitr.

How a General Command Creates Obligation: Zakat al-Fitr for the Poor
Presents differing madhhab views on zakat al-fitr obligation for the poor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi interpretation of the general command in the hadith of Tha'labah ibn Abi Sa'ir leads to the obligation of zakat al-fitr on the poor, and contrast it with the Shafi'i restriction based on their respective usul principles.

Core Idea
A general command binds all included unless a restrictor is proven.

This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: does a general command (amr) in a hadith obligate all Muslims, including the poor, or is it restricted to those who can afford it? The Hanafi school argues that the general command to pay zakat al-fitr includes anyone with surplus beyond the day's needs, while the Shafi'i school restricts it to those with surplus beyond yearly needs. This difference stems from how each school approaches the scope of general texts (umum al-nass). In this lesson, we will dissect the hadith, trace the Hanafi istinbat, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed text
The hadith quoted is: 'Pay zakat al-fitr...' (command). In Hanafi usul, this is a general (amm) command. Note that the hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalala is also zanni.
The full hadith is not given but it is a command to pay.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle on umum
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub 'ala kull man yashmaluhu al-lafz (the command indicates obligation on everyone the word includes). There is no evidence to restrict the command to the rich only. Therefore, anyone who has surplus (beyond the day's food) must pay.
Hanafis consider the command unconditional.
3
Identify the Shafi'i restriction
Shafi'is restrict the command to those who have surplus beyond yearly needs (or the needs of themselves and dependents for a year). They argue that the command is understood to apply only to those who can afford it, based on customary understanding (urf) or other evidences.
The Shafi'i position is based on a different usul approach.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a poor person who has exactly one sa' surplus for the day must pay zakat al-fitr according to Hanafi, but not according to Shafi'i. This difference is purely due to how each madhhab interprets the scope of the command.
This is a tangible example of how usul differences affect fiqh.
5
Evaluate the hadith's impact
The hadith is a single (ahad) narration, which makes its transmission zanni. Despite this, Hanafis accept it as sufficient for obligation. Shafi'is may require a higher level of certainty or additional evidence. This highlights the role of epistemological weight in istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث الاختلاف في السند والمتن ۲۹۱ لا أخرى: «إنها واجبة على المحتاج أيضاً»، وفي رواية: «إن من له أخذ زكاة الفطر فهي تجب عليه، وفي رواية مشهورة عنه: «إن زكاة الفطر واجبة على الفقير الذي يفضل عن قوته وقوت من يمونه صاع كوجوبها على الغني»). وذهب الشافعي إلى أنه من كان عنده فضل عن قوته وقوت من يمونه، وما يوفي به زكاته أداها عنه وعنهم، وإن لم يَكُنْ عنده إلا ما يؤدي عن بعضهم أداها عن بعضهم، وإن لم يكن عنده سوى مؤونته ومؤونتهم في يومه فليس عليه ولا على من يمونه زكاة (٢). وذهب إلى ذلك علي وأبو هريرة، وعطاء، وابن سيرين)، وأبو سليمان(4)، وهي إحدى الروايات عن مالك كما تقدم. وذهب الإمام أحمد إلى أن زكاة الفطر واجبة على كل من تلزمه مؤونة نفسه إذا فضل عنده عن قوته وقوت عياله يوم قوته، وقوت عياله يوم العيد، وليلته صاعاً من أي صنف تجوز الزكاة منه، فإن لم يفضل عنده إلا أقل من صاع فيؤديه في إحدى الروايتين عنه، وفي الرواية الأخرى لا تجب عليه زكاة الفطر ( ). وذهب عبيد الله بن الحسن إلى أن من أصاب فضلاً عن غدائه وعشائه فعليه أن يأخذ ويعطي صدقة الفطر ). وهنا يأتي دور حديث ابن أبي صعير السابق الذكر والتفصيل؛ فَهُوَ حجة لِمَنْ أوجب الصدقة عَلَى الفقير ؛ قال ابن قدامة: ولنا ما روي عن ثعلبة بن أبي صعير، عن أبيه؛ أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «أدوا صدقة الفطر ...».
Chapter Three: Difference in Chain and Text. 291. No other: 'Indeed it is obligatory on the needy as well,' and in one narration: 'Whoever receives zakat al-fitr must pay it,' and in a well-known narration from him: 'Zakat al-fitr is obligatory on the poor who has surplus beyond his own food and the food of his dependents equal to a sa', just as it is obligatory on the rich.' Al-Shafi'i held that whoever has surplus beyond his own food and his dependents' food, and enough to pay his zakat, he pays it for himself and them; if he has only enough to pay for some of them, he pays for some; if he has only his own and their daily subsistence, then neither he nor his dependents owe zakat. This view was also held by Ali, Abu Huraira, Ata', Ibn Sirin, Abu Sulayman, and it is one of the narrations from Malik as mentioned. Imam Ahmad held that zakat al-fitr is obligatory on everyone who is responsible for their own maintenance if they have surplus beyond their own food and their dependents' food on the day of Eid and its night, a sa' of any type that is permissible for zakat; if they have less than a sa', they pay it according to one narration, and in the other narration it is not obligatory. Ubaydullah ibn al-Hasan held that whoever has surplus beyond their lunch and dinner must take and give zakat al-fitr. Here comes the role of the hadith of Ibn Abi Sa'ir mentioned previously and in detail; it is evidence for those who make it obligatory on the poor. Ibn Qudamah said: We have what is narrated from Tha'labah ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Pay zakat al-fitr...'
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a comparative review of the different scholarly positions on whether zakat al-fitr is obligatory upon the poor. It begins by quoting Abu Hanifa's view that it is obligatory on the needy as well, even on the poor who have a surplus beyond their own and dependents' daily food. Then it mentions al-Shafi'i's view that it is only obligatory on those who have surplus beyond their own and dependents' subsistence, and if they only have enough for the day, no zakat is due. It notes that this view was also held by several Companions and early scholars, and is one of Malik's narrations. Next, it presents Imam Ahmad's view, which distinguishes between having a full sa' surplus versus less. Then it mentions the view of Ubaydullah ibn al-Hasan. Finally, it introduces the hadith of Tha'labah ibn Abi Sa'ir from his father as evidence for those who obligate it on the poor, quoting Ibn Qudamah's argument that this hadith is the proof. The passage uses this fiqh dispute as an example of how differences in hadith texts (here the hadith commanding payment) lead to differences in legal rulings among schools.

Key Terms
زكاة الفطر (zakāt al-fiṭr): A charity obligatory at the end of Ramadan, paid before Eid prayer.
فقير (faqīr): A person who does not possess surplus beyond their basic needs.
وجوب (wujūb): Legal obligation; here contrasted with being merely recommended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, when is zakat al-fitr obligatory on a poor person?
A) Always, regardless of their financial situation.
B) Only if they have surplus beyond their own and dependents' food for the day.
C) Never, because they are poor and cannot pay.
D) Only if they are the head of a household.

Key Takeaway
Differences in interpreting the generality of a command (umum al-nass) lead to different rulings on zakat al-fitr for the poor.

Textual Disruption in Hadith: Definition and Conditions
Definition and conditions of textual disruption in hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-iḍṭirāb fī al-matn and identify the three conditions that must be present for it to weaken a hadith: takaāfu' al-riwāyāt, impossibility of al-jam‘, and impossibility of al-tarjīḥ.

Core Idea
Textual disruption is a defect only when narrations are equal, cannot be combined or preferred.

In the study of hadith criticism, disruption (iḍṭirāb) is a key defect that can affect either the chain (sanad) or the text (matn). The previous section covered disruption in the chain. This lesson focuses on textual disruption: when narrators differ on the wording of a hadith in a way that cannot be resolved through harmonisation or preference. Understanding its precise conditions is essential for evaluating hadith authenticity and for usul al-fiqh, where weak hadiths are generally not used as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its scope
The term is 'al-iḍṭirāb fī al-matn'. It applies to differences in the text of a hadith, not the chain. Recognize that disruption can occur in either part.
2
Understand the three conditions for damaging disruption
The text states three conditions: (1) takaāfu' al-riwāyāt (the narrations are equal in strength), (2) impossibility of al-jam‘ (combining the narrations), (3) impossibility of al-tarjīḥ (preferring one narration). All three must be present.
If any condition is missing, the hadith is not considered muḍṭarib.
3
Distinguish between harmless and harmful differences
Not every difference causes weakness. If the narrations can be combined or one is clearly stronger, there is no disruption. Also, if the conflict is between reliable and weak narrators, the reliable ones are followed.
Ibn Daqiq al-'Id's quote reinforces: the weaker narration does not prevent acting on the stronger.
4
Apply the definition to a scenario
Consider two narrations of a hadith from the same companion: one says 'the Prophet forbade X', the other says 'the Prophet permitted X'. If both are equally reliable and cannot be reconciled, then this is textual disruption. If one is clearly more reliable, the weaker is dismissed.
Practice applying the conditions.
5
Conclude the legal effect
A hadith with textual disruption is weakened (da'īf) and cannot be used as evidence in fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني الاضطراب في المتن سبق الكلام أن الاضطراب نوعان: اضطراب يقع في السند، واضطراب يقع في المتن، وقد شرحت الاضطراب الذي يعتري الأسانيد. أما هنا فسيكون الكلام على النوع الثاني وهو الاضطراب في المتن؛ إذ كَمَا أن الاضطراب يَكُون في سند الْحَدِيث. فكذلك يَكُون في متنه. وذلك إذا وردنا حَدِيث اختلف الرُّوَاة في متنه اختلافاً لا يمكن الجمع بَيْنَ رواياته المختلفة، ولا يمكن ترجيح إحدى الروايات على البقية، فهذا يعد اضطراباً قادحاً في صحة الْحَدِيث، أما إذا أمكن الجمع فَلاً اضطراب، وكذا إذا أمكن ترجيح إحدى الروايات عَلَى البقية، فلا اضطراب إذن فالراجحة محفوظة (١) أو معروفة (٢) والمرجوحة شاذة (۳) أو منكرة (٤). وإذا كان المخالف ضعيفاً فلا تعل رِوَايَة الثقات برواية الضعفاء) فمن شروط الاضطراب تكافؤ الروايات). وقد لا يضر الاختلاف إذا كان من عدة رواة عن النبي ؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قَد يذكر الْجَمِيع، ويخبر كُلّ راوِ بِمَا حفظه عن النَّبِي ، وَلَيْسَ كُلّ اختلاف يوجب الضعف (۸) إنما الاضطراب الَّذِي يوجب الضعف هُوَ عِنْدَ اتحاد المدار، وتكافؤ الروايات، وعدم إمكان الجمع، فإذا حصل هذا فهو اضطراب مضعف للحديث، يومئ إلى عدم حفظ هذا الراوي أو الرواة لهذا الحديث قال ابن دقيق العيد: «إذا اختلفت الروايات، وكانت الحجة ببعضها دون بعض توقف الاحتجاج بشرط تعادل الروايات أما إذا وقع الترجيح لبعضها؛ بأن يكون رواتها أكثر عدداً أو أتقن حفظاً فيتعين العمل بالراجح، إذ الأضعف لا يكون مانعاً من العمل بالأقوى، والمرجوح لا يمنع التمسك بالراجح»).
Section Two: Disruption in the Text. Earlier discussion stated that disruption is of two types: disruption occurring in the chain, and disruption occurring in the text. We have explained the disruption that affects chains. Now the discussion will be on the second type, which is disruption in the text. For just as disruption occurs in the chain of a hadith, similarly it occurs in its text. This happens when we encounter a hadith whose narrators differ in its text in a way that it is not possible to combine between the different narrations, nor can one narration be preferred over the others. This is considered a damaging disruption to the hadith's authenticity. But if combination is possible, then there is no disruption. Similarly if it is possible to prefer one narration over the others, then there is no disruption; the preponderant narration is 'preserved' or 'known', and the weak one is 'irregular' or 'denied'. If the opponent is weak, the reliable narrators' narration is not tainted by the weak narrators' narration. So among the conditions of disruption is equivalence of narrations. Difference may not harm if it comes from several narrators from the Prophet, because the Prophet may have mentioned all, and each narrator reports what he heard from the Prophet. Not every difference causes weakness. The disruption that causes weakness is when the transmission is from a single source, the narrations are equivalent, and combination is impossible. When this happens, it is a weakening disruption, indicating lack of memory of this narrator or narrators for this hadith. Ibn Daqiq al-'Id said: 'If narrations differ, and the proof lies with some of them but not others, then the reliance upon them is suspended on condition of equivalence of narrations. But if preference for some occurs, because their narrators are more numerous or more precise in memory, then action upon the preponderant is determined, for the weaker does not prevent acting upon the stronger, and the outweighed does not prevent adhering to the preponderant.'
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'al-iḍṭirāb fī al-matn' (textual disruption) in hadith criticism. It begins by stating that disruption can occur in either the chain (sanad) or the text (matn). The focus here is on textual disruption. The passage then defines it: when narrators differ on the text of a hadith in such a way that the narrations cannot be harmonized (jama') nor can one be given preference (tarjīḥ) over the others, then this is considered a defect that weakens the hadith. However, if the narrations can be combined or one can be preferred, there is no disruption. The passage also mentions that if the conflicting narrator is weak, the reliable narrations are not affected. A key condition is that the conflicting narrations must be of equal strength (takaāfu' al-riwāyāt). The passage further clarifies that not every difference in wording constitutes disruption; for example, if several companions report different parts of a longer statement from the Prophet, there is no problem. The real disruptive iḍṭirāb is when all narrations come from a single source (i.e., the same narrator) but differ irreconcilably, indicating that the narrator's memory is flawed. The passage concludes with a quote from Ibn Daqiq al-'Id emphasizing that if preference is possible, action upon the preponderant narration is required. This is a foundational usul concept for evaluating hadith authenticity.

Key Terms
الاضطراب (al-iḍṭirāb): A discrepancy among narrations that cannot be resolved by combination or preference, indicating poor memory.
المتن (al-matn): The text content of the hadith, as opposed to the chain of narrators.
تكافؤ الروايات (takaāfu' al-riwāyāt): The condition that the conflicting narrations are equal in strength (number or reliability of narrators).
الجمع (al-jam‘): The process of harmonizing different narrations to resolve apparent contradiction.
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Giving preference to one narration over another based on criteria like number of narrators or precision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is a necessary condition for a hadith to be considered muḍṭarib (disrupted) in its text?
A) All narrations are weak.
B) The narrations are equivalent in strength and cannot be reconciled nor preferred.
C) There are multiple narrations from different companions.
D) The hadith contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Textual disruption is a defect only when the conflicting narrations are equivalent, cannot be harmonized, and cannot be preferred; otherwise, the hadith remains valid evidence.

Idtirab in the Matn: Conditions and Application
Defines harmful idtirab in matn with conditions and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify harmful idtirab in a hadith matn and distinguish it from harmless textual variation using the conditions of equivalence, unity of focus, and irreconcilability.

Core Idea
Not every textual variation weakens a hadith; only irreconcilable differences among equally strong narrations constitute harmful idtirab.

This passage from a principles of hadith work explains the second type of disruption (idtirab) — that which occurs in the text of a hadith. Understanding the precise conditions for harmful idtirab is crucial for evaluating the authenticity of narrations. Through the example of the 'wahibat nafsaha' hadith, we see how seemingly different wordings can be reconciled, preserving the hadith's authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Define harmful idtirab in matn
Identify the definition from the passage: textual difference among narrators that cannot be reconciled (jam') or preferred (tarjih). Note that harmless differences are those where reconciliation or preference is possible.
The passage distinguishes between harmful and harmless idtirab.
2
List the three necessary conditions
From the passage and the quotes from Ibn Hajar and others, identify: (1) equivalence of narrations (takaafu al-riwayat), (2) unity of focus (ittihad al-madar), and (3) impossibility of reconciliation (al-jam') or preference (al-tarjih).
All three must be present for idtirab to be harmful.
3
Understand the role of jam' and tarjih
If the narrations can be harmonized (jam') or one is clearly stronger (tarjih), there is no harmful idtirab. Preference can be based on number of narrators, memorization, etc.
The passage clarifies that the weaker narration does not affect the stronger one.
4
Apply the conditions to the example hadith
Examine the hadith of al-wahibat nafsaha. Note the variations in wording (zawwajtuka, ankahuha, mallaktuka, etc.). Determine if these variations meet the conditions for harmful idtirab.
The variations are synonymous and can be reconciled; hence the hadith remains authentic.
5
Summarize the practical rule
Conclude that only when the variations are contradictory, of equal strength, and cannot be resolved should a hadith be deemed weak due to idtirab.
This rule is applied by Hanafi and other hadith scholars.

(۱) وهي رواية الثقة إذا خالفها الثقة الأقل حفظاً أو عدداً. (۲) وهي رواية الثقة التي خالفها الضعيف. (۳) وهي رواية الثقة التي خالفها من هو أوثق عدداً أو حفظاً. (٤) وهي رواية الضعيف التي خالفت الثقات. Chapter Three: Disagreement in Chain and Text. Section Two: Disruption in the Text. Previously it was said that disruption is of two types: disruption in the chain and disruption in the text. The disruption that affects chains has been explained. As for here, it will be about the second type, which is disruption in the text. Just as disruption can occur in the chain of a hadith, so too can it occur in its text. This happens when we find a hadith whose narrators differed in its text in a way that it is impossible to reconcile between the different narrations, and it is impossible to prefer one narration over the rest. This is considered harmful disruption that affects the hadith's authenticity. But if reconciliation is possible, then there is no disruption. Similarly, if it is possible to prefer one narration over the rest, then there is no disruption. The preferred narration is either preserved (1) or known (2), and the non-preferred is anomalous (3) or rejected (4). If the opposing narrator is weak, then the narration of reliable narrators is not affected by the narration of weak ones. Among the conditions of disruption is equivalence of narrations. Difference may not be harmful if it comes from multiple narrators from the Prophet (peace be upon him) because the Prophet may have mentioned all, and each narrator reported what he memorized from the Prophet. Not all difference necessitates weakness; rather, the disruption that necessitates weakness is when there is unity of focus, equivalence of narrations, and impossibility of reconciliation. If this occurs, it is a disruption that weakens the hadith, indicating lack of memorization of this narrator or narrators. Ibn Daqiq al-Eid said: 'If narrations differ, and the proof is with some without others, using the evidence is suspended pending equivalence of narrations. But if preference occurs for some—because their narrators are more numerous or more precise in memorization—then action upon the preferred is necessary, for the weaker does not prevent action upon the stronger, and the non-preferred does not prevent holding fast to the preferred.' (1) It is the narration of a reliable narrator when opposed by a less reliable in memorization or number. (2) It is the narration of a reliable narrator opposed by a weak one. (3) It is the narration of a reliable narrator opposed by one more reliable in number or memorization. (4) It is the narration of a weak narrator that opposed the reliable ones. (5) Fath al-Bari 3/213 (6) Fath al-Bari 5/318 (7) See: Tahrir al-Tathrib 2/30 (8) Hadi al-Sari: 347 (9) Fath al-Bari 5/318. Al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: 'Difference among the memorizers of hadith does not necessitate that it be considered disrupted except with two conditions: one being the equivalence of the aspects of difference; so whenever one of the statements is given preference, it is advanced and the correct is not affected by the non-preferred. The second: given equivalence, that reconciliation be impossible according to the principles of hadith scholars, and it is most likely that that memorizer did not precisely retain that particular hadith. Then that narration alone is judged to be disrupted, and judgment on the authenticity of that hadith is suspended for that reason. (1) Hadi al-Sari: 348-349 (2) Tuhfat al-Ahwadhi 2/91-92 (3) He is Salamah ibn Dinar, Abu Hazim al-A'raj al-Tammar al-Madani, client of al-Aswad ibn Sufyan, reliable, devout, died in the caliphate of al-Mansur. Tahdhib al-Kamal 3/244, (2434), and al-Taqrib (2489). (4) Ibn Hajar stated that in Nukat 'ala Ibn al-Salah 2/808 (5) Narrated by Malik (411) in the transmission of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Qasim, (318) in the transmission of Suwayd ibn Sa'id, (1477) in the transmission of Abu Mus'ab al-Zuhri with the wording 'zawwajtuka', and (1498) in the transmission of al-Laithi with the wording 'ankahnikaha'. Al-Laithi alone opposed the companions of Malik. It was also narrated by al-Shafi'i in Musnad (1117), Ahmad 5/336, al-Bukhari 3/132 (2310), 7/22 (5135), 9/151 (7417), Abu Dawud (2111), al-Tirmidhi (1114), al-Nasa'i 6/123 and in al-Kubra (5524), al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar 3/16, Ibn Hibban (4093), al-Bayhaqi 7/144, 236, 242, and al-Baghawi (2302) all of them narrated from Malik with the wording 'qad zawwajtukaha'. Narrated by al-Darimi (2207), al-Bukhari 6/26 (5029) from 'Amr ibn 'Awn with the wording 'zawwajtukaha'.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage from the book's third chapter, second section, explains the concept of 'al-Idtirab fi al-Matan' (disruption in the text of hadith). It builds on the earlier discussion of disruption in the chain. The author defines harmful idtirab as a difference among narrators in the text that cannot be resolved through reconciliation (jama') or preference (tarjih). If reconciliation or preference is possible, there is no harmful disruption; the preferred narration is considered preserved or known, while the non-preferred is anomalous or rejected. A key condition is that the conflicting narrations must be equivalent in strength (takaafu al-riwayat); if one side is clearly weaker, it does not harm the other. The passage also clarifies that not all textual differences cause weakness; only those that meet the condition of unity of focus (ittihad al-madar), equivalence, and irreconcilability. The author quotes Ibn Daqiq al-Eid and Ibn Hajar to support these conditions. Then an example is given: the hadith of the woman who gifted herself in marriage (al-wahibat nafsaha) narrated by Abu Hazim from Sahl ibn Sa'd. Different narrators used different verbs: 'zawwajtuka', 'ankahuka', 'mallaktuka', etc. Despite these differences, scholars do not consider this harmful idtirab because all these verbs convey the same meaning of effecting marriage, and the differences can be reconciled as variations in expression. The detailed footnotes list the various chains and wordings, all ultimately pointing to the same core meaning. This analysis shows how the principles of idtirab are applied in practice: the differences here are not contradictory but merely lexical, so the hadith remains authentic.

Key Terms
الاضطراب (al-idtirab): Disruption or confusion in the text or chain of a hadith due to irreconcilable differences among equivalent narrations.
تكافؤ الروايات (takaafu al-riwayat): Equal strength of conflicting narrations in terms of number of narrators and reliability, such that no clear preference is possible.
الجمع (al-jam'): Reconciliation or harmonization of different narrations to remove apparent contradiction.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): Preferring one narration over another based on criteria like more narrators, stronger memory, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a condition for a textual difference to be considered harmful idtirab?
A) The narrators must be from different regions.
B) The conflicting narrations must be equivalent in strength and cannot be reconciled or one preferred.
C) The difference must involve a legal ruling (hukm).
D) The hadith must be narrated by only one narrator.

Key Takeaway
Harmful idtirab requires equivalence of narrations, unity of focus, and impossibility of reconciliation or preference; mere textual variation does not weaken a hadith.

Conditions for Mudtarib and the Hadith of al-Wahiba Nafsaha
Stating conditions for mudtarib and illustrating with hadith of al-wahiba nafsaha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a hadith to be classified as mudtarib according to Ibn Hajar, and apply them to a hadith with multiple wordings to determine whether it is mudtarib.

Core Idea
A hadith is mudtarib only when conflicting versions are equal and irreconcilable; mere variation does not impair.

This lesson examines a key principle in hadith criticism: when does variation among narrators render a hadith 'confused' (mudtarib)? You will learn Ibn Hajar's two conditions and see them applied to the famous hadith of the woman who offered herself in marriage. The goal is to understand that not every difference in wording affects the authenticity; only when versions are equally strong and cannot be reconciled does the narration become suspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and Its Sources
Find the statements of Ibn Hajar and al-Mubarakfuri in the passage. Note that they both emphasize that mere difference does not necessitate mudtarib.
Ibn Hajar is from Hadi al-Sari; al-Mubarakfuri from Tuhfat al-Ahwadhi.
2
Extract the Two Conditions
From Ibn Hajar's definition, identify the two conditions: (1) the conflicting versions must be equal in strength (no preponderance), and (2) reconciliation must be impossible according to hadith rules, leading to doubt about the narrator's memory.
If one version is preponderant (rajih), the hadith is ruled sahih based on that version.
3
Apply Conditions to the Example Hadith
Review the many wordings of the hadith of al-wahiba nafsaha (e.g., zawwajtukaha, ankahtukaha, mallaktukaha). Determine whether the two conditions are met. Notice that the variants are not equal in strength because each is supported by multiple chains, and they can be reconciled as synonyms for marriage.
The passage explicitly says this difference 'does not impair according to the scholars due to the lack of complete contradiction'.
4
Conclude the Ruling on the Hadith
Since the conditions for mudtarib are not satisfied, the hadith is not ruled mudtarib. It remains sahih (authentic) if its chain is otherwise sound.
This illustrates that not every variation leads to weakness.

And al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar said: Difference (ikhtilaf) among the memorizers (huffaz) concerning a hadith does not necessitate that it be mudtarib (confused/dubious) except with two conditions: One of them is the equivalence of the aspects of difference; whenever one of the statements is preponderant (rajih), it is given precedence, and the sahih is not declared defective by the marjuh. The second: along with equivalence, that reconciliation (jama') is impossible according to the rules of the muhaddithin, and it becomes the predominant opinion that that memorizer did not precisely preserve that particular hadith; then at that point judgment is passed on that single narration as being mudtarib, and one refrains from judging the hadith as sahih because of that. And al-Mubarakfuri said: It has been established in the principles of hadith that mere difference does not necessitate mudtarab, rather one of its conditions is the equivalence of the aspects of difference; whenever one of the statements is preponderant, it is given precedence. And there may be difference and no preponderance possible, yet it is a difference that does not impair according to the scholars due to the lack of complete contradiction, such as the hadith of al-wahiba nafsaha (the woman who gave herself [in marriage]). It is what Abu Hazim narrated from Sahl ibn Sa'd, who said: A woman came to the Messenger of Allah and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I have given myself to you.' Then a man said, 'Marry her to me.' He said, 'We have married her to you with what you have of the Qur'an.' This hadith is unique to Abu Hazim, and the narrators differed from him concerning it: some of them said 'Ankahtukaha' (I gave her to you in marriage), some said 'Zawwajtukaha' (I married her to you), some said 'Mallaktukaha' (I gave her to you in possession), some said 'Mulliktaha' (she was given to you in possession), some said 'Zawwajnaka' (We married you), some said 'Fa zawwajahu' (so he married him), some said 'Ankahtuka' (I gave you in marriage), some said 'Amallaktuka' (I gave you possession), some said 'Amallaktukaha' (I gave you possession of her), some said 'Zawwajtuka' (I married you), and the explanation of that is in the footnote. (1) Hadi al-Sari: 348-349 (2) Tuhfat al-Ahwadhi 2/91-92 (3) He is Salama ibn Dinar, Abu Hazim al-Araj al-Tammar al-Madani, mawla of al-Aswad ibn Sufyan, trustworthy, devout, died during the caliphate of al-Mansur. Tahdhib al-Kamal 3/244 (2434), al-Taqrib (2489). (4) Ibn Hajar stated this in his Nukat 'ala Ibn al-Salah 2/808. Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a hadith methodology (usul al-hadith) rule concerning the classification of a hadith as mudtarib (confused). The author first quotes Ibn Hajar al-'Asqalani, who states that merely having differences among memorizers (huffaz) in narrating a hadith does not automatically make it mudtarib. Two conditions must be met: (1) the different versions must be equal in strength, meaning none is preponderant (rajih) over another; if one can be preferred, the hadith is ruled as sahih based on the preponderant version, and the weaker version does not defect the sahih. (2) Even when equal, it must be impossible to reconcile the versions according to the rules of hadith scholars, and it becomes likely that the memory of a particular narrator was faulty. Only then is that specific narration deemed mudtarib, and judgment on the hadith's authenticity is suspended. Then al-Mubarakfuri is quoted affirming the same principle: mere difference does not necessitate mudtarab; it requires equivalence and irreconcilability. As an example, the hadith of al-wahiba nafsaha (the woman who gave herself in marriage) is cited. Despite numerous variant wordings from different chains—all going back to Abu Hazim from Sahl ibn Sa'd—the hadith is not ruled mudtarib because the differences are not equal in strength (many are supported by multiple chains) and can often be reconciled as synonyms for marriage. The bulk of the passage is a detailed listing of the many narrators and their wordings, demonstrating the vast transmission network and the variety of expressions used. This illustrates that not every variation leads to weakness; scholars accept such variations when the core meaning is consistent and when the differences do not imply contradiction.

Key Terms
مضطرب (mudtarib): A hadith narrated with different versions that are equal in strength and cannot be reconciled, leading to doubt about the narrator's precision.
الاضطراب (al-Idtirab): The state of being mudtarib; having conflicting equal versions.
الواهبة نفسها (al-wahiba nafsaha): The woman who offered herself in marriage to the Prophet (hadith).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Hajar, when does a difference in narrations (ikhtilaf) make a hadith mudtarib?
A) Whenever there is any difference in wording, the hadith becomes mudtarib.
B) Only when the differences are equal in strength and cannot be reconciled, and it seems the narrator did not preserve the hadith accurately.
C) Only when the differences involve contradictions in the matn, not the sanad.
D) Only when the hadith is narrated by a single narrator from beginning to end.

Key Takeaway
A hadith is mudtarib only when conflicting versions are equal in strength and irreconcilable; mere variation does not impair authenticity.
